

2008 WL 4420136 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOMASZ DOMAGALSKI
File: AXXX XX9 855 - New York, NY
September 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Noemi E. Masliah, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Adam P. Feller
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on July 17, 2001, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our previous March 30, 2001, decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s February 1, 2000, denial of his motion to reopen the respondent’s December 7, 1998, in absentia order. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen on May 19, 2008. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be granted pursuant to our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&NDec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
In his current motion, as in his original motion to reconsider which we denied on March 30, 2001,¿FN1º the respondent argues that an in absentia order should not have been issued against him because he appeared at the hearing 45 minutes late due to circumstances beyond his control. He elaborates, as he did in his original motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, that his subway train was delayed and that once he arrived at the courthouse, he was misdirected to the courtroom. Morever, for the first time, his current motion asserts that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at this hearing from two prior attorneys, who failed to properly file a timely motion to reopen his in absentia hearing, and he was thereby precluded from presenting his applications for withholding of removal and voluntary departure. For its part, the DHS argues that the respondent’s description of the procedures at the courthouse are inaccurate, and that the respondent failed to submit any evidence in support of his claim that his subway train was late. The DHS does not address the respondent’s claim regarding the effectiveness of his prior counsels.
 
As an initial matter, we agree that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former counselors. Subsequent to the in absentia order issued by the Immigration Judge, Ms. Reede, Esquire, informed the respondent that she would file a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, but such motion was ultimately filed late and denied by the Immigration Judge on February 1, 2000. Inasmuch as Ms. Reede’s motion was late and deficient on its face, we can presume that the respondent was prejudiced thereby. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, we find that the respondent’s second attorney, Mr. Oeser-Sweate, Esquire, failed to properly file an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his former counsel, and the lack of such a motion further prevented the respondent from presenting his requests for relief from removal. Id. In support of the respondent’s allegations, the respondent has submitted, inter alia, a copy of a motion prepared by Mr. Oeser-Sweate, outlining the ineffective nature of his prior counsel, and his accompanying affidavit, he alleges that this motion was improperly filed with the New York ICE office but never with the Immigration Court.¿FN2º The respondent has filed disciplinary complaints against Mr. Oeser-Sweate and Mr. Murado,¿FN3º and has substantially met the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has stated that “¿iºn a situation where ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from having the opportunity to present his case for relief, the filing deadline for motions to reopen will be equitably tolled until the ineffective assistance is, or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.” See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 171 (2d Cir. 2006); citing Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000); see also Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 157 (2d Cir. 2006). In this case, we find that the respondent has adequately explained that his prior counsels prevented him from presenting his applications for relief, and in delaying his ability to file an ineffective assistance of counsel alleging this fact. The respondent, through current counsel, effectively sets forth this ineffective assistance of counsel claim with due diligence, inasmuch as it attempts to remedy Mr. Oeser-Sweate’s and Ms. Reede’s errors. See Cekic v. INS, supra, at 170 (“no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled”). Specifically, the respondent has explained that his delay in the current motion resulted from the misleading assurances he received from Ms. Reede, and the inadequate representation provided by her and by Mr. Oeser-Sweate.
 
Lastly, we note that the Immigration Judge, in his February 1, 2000, denial of the respondent’s initial motion to reopen his in absentia hearing, held that even were the motion timely, he would still deny it because the respondent’s late train does not constitute “exceptional circumstances” to excuse his absence at the hearing (2000 I.J. Dec. at 3). However, were it not for Ms. Reede and Mr. Murado’s inability to appear at the hearing at the proper time, and their failure to properly guide the respondent through the process once he arrived at the Immigration Court, he may have avoided an in absentia removal order. In this vein, we note that recent case law out of United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has held that such short delays may not be considered a “failure to appear” for purposes of in absentia hearings. See Abu Hasirah v. DHS, 478 F.3d 474 (2d Cir. 2007). In short, given the circumstances, we find that the motions deadline should be excused under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, supra. Accordingly, the record is remanded in order to afford the respondent an opportunity to pursue his application for withholding of removal before the Immigration Judge, as well as his more recent request to apply for adjustment of status based on an approved Form I-130 visa petition.
 
*3 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion to reconsider, which we denied on July 17, 2001, merely argued that we “rubber-stamped” the Immigration Judge’s December 7, 1998, in our March 30, 2001, decision. It does not directly address the underlying in absentia decision, but in effect requests that the Board reconsider the basis of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order.

2

We note, incidentally, that the motion posits the same claims present in the pending motion to reopen.

3

The respondent has indicated that Ms. Reede is now deceased. Mr. Murado, Esquire, assisted Ms. Reede in her representation of the respondent.

2008 WL 4420133 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JEOSEPH SELLOUM GOUL A.K.A. JOSEPH GOUL
File: AXXX XX9 639 - Boston, MA
September 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Obadan Iziokhai, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s order dated November 16, 2007. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The facts and pertinent history of the case are as follows. On September 1, 2006, the respondent filed (with the Immigration Court) his Application to Adjust Status (Form I-485) and supporting documents, including an approval notice for the visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen spouse. On July 27, 2007, the Immigration Court sent a hearing notice for October 30, 2007, to the respondent’s former counsel (Gordie Daniels). The respondent did not appear for the October 30, 2007, hearing and the Immigration Judge subsequently entered an in absentia order.
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen on November 5, 2007. The respondent’s motion was supported by his declaration made under the penalty of perjury. The respondent stated in his declaration that on or about October 25, 2007, present counsel (Obadan Iziokhai) informed the respondent he would represent him at the next hearing because Mr. Daniels was out of the country. The respondent stated that Mr. Iziokhai informed him the hearing was set for October 31, 2007. The respondent stated Mr. Iziokhai told him that he could not find the respondent’s file at Mr. Daniel’s office so he contacted the Immigration Court and was told by a clerk the hearing was scheduled for October 31, 2007.¿FN1º The respondent stated that he and his wife appeared for the hearing at the Immigration Court on October 31, 2007, and were told the hearing had been conducted on October 30, 2007. The respondent also submitted in support of his motion a letter from his employer which stated that he had taken off from work on October 31, 2007, specifically to attend the hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion in a brief order dated November 16, 2007 (and mailed on November 26, 2007). The respondent timely appealed.
 
Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), provides, in pertinent part, an in absentia order may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the removal order if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances.
 
We find that the respondent has shown exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear at the October 30, 2007, hearing. In Kaweesa v. Gonzales, 450 F.3d 62, 68 (1st Cir. 2006), the court stated that in deciding the validity of a claim of exceptional circumstances, the totality of the circumstances must be considered. Here the respondent appeared for the hearing the next day (October 31, 2007), obtained a letter from his employer which stated he took off work on October 31, 2007, specifically to attend the hearing, and promptly filed a motion to reopen supported by his declaration and the employer letter on November 5, 2007. We note that the respondent appears eligible for adjustment of status, based on an approved immediate relative visa petition filed on his behalf. Although the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of counsel have not been met, it is clear from the record that the respondent did not appear for the October 30, 2007, hearing because of misinformation from Mr. Iziokhai. We held in Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996), that ineffective assistance of counsel could constitute exceptional circumstances to rescind an in absentia order. We noted that an alien is not required to show prejudice in order to rescind an in absentia order. Id, at 473 n.2. We thus conclude that the respondent has shown exceptional circumstances.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decision is reversed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

It is not clear whether Mr. Iziokhai misheard or the Immigration Court clerk misstated the date in question.

2008 WL 4420138 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICOLAS KING A.K.A. NICHOLAS KING
File: AXXX XX5 026 - Boston, MA
September 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth Finn, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on May 20, 2008, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 6, 2008, decision ordering him removed from the United States to Trinidad and Tobago. The respondent has now, on August 15, 2008, filed a timely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence sought to be offered “was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” See 8C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Further, this Board has held that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue a discretionary grant of relief from removal bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if his motion to reopen were granted, the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). In the respondent’s case, we do not find the evidence in support of his motion material. Unfortunately, regardless of the respondent’s letters of support, he remains ineligible for cancellation of removal due to his aggravated felony conviction. As we explained in our prior May 20, 2008, decision, his 2005 conviction for possession with intent to distribute cocaine bars him from such relief under section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. While the respondent now argues in his motion that he should not be considered an aggravated felon pursuant to the United States Supreme Court holding in Lopez v. Gonzalez, 127 S.Ct. 625 (2006), we explained in Matter of Aruna, 24 I&N Dec. 452 (BIA 2008) that even though the respondent may only have been sentenced to 364 days, his conviction is an aggravated felony because a state drug offense which corresponds to a Federal felony qualifies as a “drug trafficking crime” aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act.
 
The respondent also requests that we reconsider our previous decision because, first, he only had sufficient drugs in his possession to be considered for “personal use,” and, secondly, because he received ineffective assistance of counsel at his criminal hearing. We can not go beyond the record of conviction to consider these arguments. See Matter of Khalik, 17 I&N Dec. 518 (BIA 1980). Accordingly, we find no reason to disturb our prior decision, and the motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4420138 (BIA)

2008 WL 4335856 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: COSIAS DUARTE-MEJIA
File: AXXX XX0 715 - Miami, FL
September 8, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Louis Gvzmann, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This is an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision of October 15, 2007, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, on the ground that he did not file his motion within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s decision of June 15, 2007, as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent had not established changed country conditions in the country to which removal has been ordered, Honduras, and that the respondent therefore failed to demonstrate that he qualified for an exception to the filing requirements. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent had not established the prerequisites needed to reopen this matter based on ineffective assistance of counsel. In addition, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion requesting a stay of his voluntary departure, on the ground that no substantial grounds had been advanced to warrant its grant. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that he filed two motions to reopen, and the Miami Immigration Court ignored his request and, failed to act, and neither denied nor approved either of the two motions. He also claims that the Immigration Court did not entertain his motion to stay his voluntary departure, or to change venue to the Orlando Immigration Court. He asserts that he is entitled to this relief so that he can have his case reopened and pursue his claim for asylum relief.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge did in fact, issue a decision dated October 15, 2007, in response to the respondent’s motions. The record also reflects that the respondent’s attorney was served with the Immigration Judge’s decision on October 16, 2007. A timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion was filed with this Board by counsel for the alien on October 21, 2007.
 
We will deny the respondent’s motion, as it failed to meet the regulatory requirements. A motion to reopen on factual grounds must be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The applicant must show that the evidence is material, unavailable at the time of the original hearing, and could not have been discovered or presented at the original hearing. Such a motion shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that these criteria are met. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Upon review of the respondent’s motion we find that he has submitted no evidentiary material, other than his own affidavit, and does not assert that there is material evidence which was unavailable and could not have been discovered at the original hearing. The respondent’s motion therefore does not meet the aforementioned regulatory criteria. We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to demonstrate that he was rendered ineffective assistance of counsel, as he did not meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and therefore a claim of ineffective assistance could not serve as a basis for the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
*2 In this case, it appears that the respondent’s motion was not timely, as the record reflects that the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered on June 15, 2007, and the respondent’s motion was not received by this Board until September 18, 2007. We note that the respondent sought a stay of his voluntary departure. However, since his motion to reopen was not timely, the recent Supreme Court decision in Dada v. Mukasey, 123 S. Ct. 2307 (2008), does not apply to this case. We find that Dada requires that, in order for the alien to be allowed to obtain the benefit of its holding, i.e., the right to unilaterally withdraw his/her voluntary departure request without regard to the merits of the motion to reopen, the alien’s motion to reopen must be timely, i.e., filed within 90 days. We note that the Court cites to the statutory provision governing the deadline for filing motions to reopen, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii). Moreover, the Court noted that its decision to allow an alien to withdraw his/her voluntary departure request and pursue his/her motion is premised, at least in part, upon the notion that it is completely foreseeable that the time allowed for voluntary departure will expire long before the BIA issues a decision on a timely filed motion to reopen. Id. at 2316. We find that this clearly shows that Dada mandates that the respondent’s motion to reopen be timely in order for him to be able to withdraw his voluntary departure request and pursue his motion. Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4335856 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 4335843 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO HOMERO SALGADO
File: AXXX XX0 668 - Fishkill, NY
September 8, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura A. Michalec
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen; termination of proceedings
 
In a decision dated January 9, 2007, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable, and ordered him removed to Ecuador. The respondent’s appeal to the Board was denied as untimely on May 14, 2007. On June 4, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s April 22, 2008, untimely motion to reopen. The respondent appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is an approximately 52-year-old native and citizen of Ecuador. The respondent entered the United States on or about April 28, 1974, as a visitor. He adjusted his status to that of a lawful permanent resident on or about August 30, 1979. On February 28, 2006, he was convicted in the Supreme Court, New York County, New York, of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree, cocaine. He was sentenced to six years’ imprisonment. He did not file a direct appeal. The Supreme Court, New York County, New York, agreed to accept his late appeal on May 15, 2007. The respondent asserts that the proceedings should be reopened, even though his motion is untimely, because his conviction is no longer final; further, his attorney did not file an appeal leading to the dismissal of his pro se appeal as untimely. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the motion because, it argues, the motion is untimely and he remains removable for the 2006 conviction. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23.
 
Arguably, the respondent has not substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He did not inform his former attorney that he was filing a complaint with the New York State Bar to give him an opportunity to respond to the allegations. Nevertheless, the motion to reopen depends on the argument that his former counsel was ineffective by failing to file an appeal. We note there is no evidence the respondent and his attorney agreed to file an appeal. The attorney was not clear that he would file an appeal at the hearing and the respondent at that time was given instructions how to file an appeal and the proper forms (Tr. at 12-15). The respondent himself subsequently filed an untimely appeal on March 28, 2007, but failed to meet the requirements and did not return the appeal until April 19, 2007. In addition, he did not show due diligence in filing his motion, failing to raise his claim until almost a year after the Board dismissed his appeal. Chen v. Gonzales, 437 F.3d 267 (2d Cir. 2006).
 
*2 Further, to show ineffective assistance of counsel prejudice must be shown. Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647 (2d Cir. 2008). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004) (alien must show outcome might have been affected). The respondent has proffered no errors in the Immigration Judge’s decision which would demonstrate that the decision could have been reversed on appeal. Thus, he has not demonstrated prejudice from the failure of his former attorney to file an appeal. The filing of a post-conviction motion with the state court is not a ground for reopening a removal proceeding. See 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48); Matter of Marroquin, 23 I&N Dec. 705 (BIA 1997; A.G. 2005); Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994); see also Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750, 751-52 (BIA 1993).
 
The respondent claims he is not removable because his request to appeal his controlled substance conviction has been granted. There is no requirement that all appeals be exhausted before a conviction is final for immigration purposes. See Puello v. Bureau of Citizenship and Immigration Services, 511 F.3d 324 (2d Cir. 2007); see also Abiodun v. Gonzales, 461 F.3d 1210 (10th Cir. 2006); Montenegro v. Ashcroft, 355 F.3d 1035 (7th Cir. 2004) (per curiam); Moosa v. INS, 171 F.3d 994 (5th Cir. 1999).
 
Insofar as the respondent argues he is not guilty of the crime of which he was convicted, the Board cannot go behind the conviction to determine guilt or innocence. Matter of Roberts, 20 I&N Dec. 294 (BIA 1991); Matter of Fortis, 14 I&N Dec. 576 (BIA 1974); see Matter of Mendez-Morales, 21 I&N Dec. 296 (BIA 1996); Matter of Reyes, 20 I&N Dec. 789 (BIA 1994). Likewise, matters involving state law are beyond the scope of our jurisdiction. The respondent must address his arguments to the state court. The respondent also argues he can apply for a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Act. The United States Supreme Court has held that the restrictions on section 212(c) relief do not apply retroactively to those aliens, who pled guilty to criminal offenses prior to April 24, 1996. See INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). The respondent was convicted on February 28, 2006, and a waiver under former section 212(c) is not available to him. As the respondent is not eligible for discretionary relief, his long residence and family ties or hardship upon removal cannot be considered.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed. ORDER: The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4335843 (BIA)

2008 WL 4335839 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TATIANA VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX9 947 - Detroit, MI
September 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael B. Dobson
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the denial of her motion to reopen in absentia removal proceedings by the Immigration Judge. She claims that she did not receive notice of her hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a request for summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. This matter will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
The Immigration Judge incorrectly applied the 180-day deadline to the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that it was based on a claim of exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at her removal hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(b)(5)(C)(i)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
We agree with the respondent’s assertion that prior counsel’s failure to file an entry of appearance, and failure to notify the Immigration Court of a change of address on behalf of the respondent, are facts that, if established, would lead to a finding that the respondent was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In addition, the record is replete with evidence of malfeasance on the part of this attorney, which may contribute to the credibility of the respondent’s assertions. Therefore, this matter will be remanded to the Immigration Judge to assess whether ineffective assistance of counsel prevented the respondent from receiving the hearing notice.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4335839 (BIA)

2008 WL 4335838 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARIEL MICHAEL PUNZALAN
File: AXXX XX2 425 - San Juan, PR
September 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa Dubowski, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; adjustment of status
 
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on August 17, 2006, when we remanded the record of proceeding to the Immigration Judge for the issuance of a new decision in accordance with Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994). The Immigration Judge subsequently rendered a new decision on September 5, 2006, and the respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, appeals from that decision presently. In support of the appeal, the respondent argues: (I) that the Immigration Judge’s September 5, 2006, decision fails identify and fully explain the reasons for the denial of the motion to reopen; and (2) that the Immigration Judge erroneously denied the motion to reopen because the ineffective assistance of the respondent’s prior counsel should have been invoked to equitably toll the time and numeric limitations on motions to reopen (Respondent’s Br. at 3-9). See generally, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (2008) (setting forth time and numeric limitations on motions to reopen filed before the Immigration Judge after entry of final administrative order). As we disagree on both counts, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
First, we find no merit to the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge’s decision is legally deficient because, as the respondent asserts, the Immigration Judge “adopted the ¿Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”)º rationale without further analysis (Respondent’s Br. at 2).” While certain portions of the Immigration Judge’s decision evidently draw upon the arguments offered in the DHS’s response in opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen filed on May 19, 2006, there is nothing in the Immigration and Nationality Act (“the Act”) or its attendant regulations that prevents the Immigration Judge from drawing upon the arguments offered by the parties to the proceeding in the course of reaching and articulating a decision. All that we require when an Immigration Judge renders a ruling on a motion to reopen is that the Immigration Judge “identify and fully explain the reasons for such decision,” and the Immigration Judge’s September 5, 2006, decision satisfies this basic adjudicative criterion. See Matter of M-P-, supra.
 
Second, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments on appeal that the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel should be invoked to equitably toll the time and numeric limitations on motions to reopen. Indeed, we are not persuaded that the respondent’s prior counsel acted ineffectively. The respondent, represented by former counsel, filed the first motion to reopen on March 17, 2006, which, as the Immigration Judge correctly observed and the respondent does not contest, was 7 days after the expiration of the respondent’s voluntary departure period on March 10, 2 007 (I.J. at 1 (Sept. 5, 2006)). Rather than take an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 29, 2006, decision denying the first motion to reopen, the respondent retained present counsel of record and filed a second motion to reopen on May 9, 2006, which is both numerically and time-barred under the governing regulatory provision, a legal conclusion that the respondent also does not contest. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The purpose of both motions to reopen was to apply for adjustment of status pursuant to Matter of Velarde-Pacheco, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) on the basis of a marriage to a United States citizen on December 19, 2005, which was 21 days prior to the entry of the final administrative order dated January 9, 2006, granting the respondent the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States on or before March 10, 2006 (I.J. at 1).
 
*2 We note that the Immigration Judge appropriately denied the respondent’s first motion to reopen because the respondent was ineligible to adjust his status as a result of his failure to voluntarily depart the United States before the expiration of the voluntary departure period. See section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. Against this backdrop we turn to the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and we observe at the outset that such claims require, among other things, “that the motion to reopen be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard.” See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 603 (BIA 1996) (emphasis added) (citing Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988)). The substance of the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is that: (1) he retained the former counsel in December of 2005 to file a motion to reopen to enable him to apply for adjustment of status (Respondent’s M.T.R., Exhs. B, C (May 9, 2006)); (2) the respondent repeatedly contacted the former counsel concerning his case, and he was assured the motion to reopen was filed on time (Respondent’s M.T.R., Exhs. B, C (May 9, 2006)); and (3) “due to an error on ¿ the former counsel’sº part, the motion was not timely filed (Respondent’s M.T.R., exhs. B, C (May 9, 2006)).”
 
Such generalized, vague, and unsupported assertions are insufficient to establish any ineffectiveness on the part of the respondent’s former counsel, particularly so when they are scrutinized in light of the evidence in the record of proceeding. The respondent’s first motion to reopen contains 5 exhibits marked A through E, all of which except for exhibit E relate to the bona fides of the underlying marriage (Respondent’s M.T.R. (March 17, 2006)). As we examine these exhibits, it is evident to us that the respondent, and not respondent’s prior counsel, must bear the chief responsibility for whatever delay there may have been in properly filing the motion before the Immigration Judge. Bearing in mind that the respondent was married on December 19, 2005, we note the following. First, the Form I-130, Petition for Alien Relative, was not received by the DHS until January 25, 2006 (Respondent’s M.T.R., exh. B (March 17, 2006)). Second, exhibit C attached to the respondent’s motion to reopen contains an assortment of letters and a bank statement bearing dates ranging February 1 to 6, 2006 (Respondent’s M.T.R., exh. C (March 17, 2006)). Third, the sine qua non of the respondent’s first motion to reopen motion, the Form I-485, Application to Register Permanent Residence or Adjust Status, shows that it was signed and dated by the respondent on March 1, 2006, and that it was apparently faxed to the respondent’s former counsel on March 2, 2006 (Respondent’s M.T.R., exh. D (March 17, 2006)). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(c) (providing that a motion to reopen filed for the purpose of applying for relief from removal must be accompanied by the requisite application). The respondent’s former counsel subsequently paid the required fee for the motion to reopen on the following day; however, the motion to reopen was not properly filed until March 17, 2006 (Respondent’s M.T.R. (March 17, 2006)). Thus, from this evidence it is clear to us that the respondent’s former counsel could only act as quickly as permitted by the respondent’s piecemeal manner of supplying him with the requisite evidentiary documents in support of the first motion to reopen. Although there is an unexplained 2-week gap between the date on which the filing fee was paid and the date on which the motion to reopen was actually filed, much of this elapsed time is irrelevant because the critical date here is March 10, 2006, the date on which the respondent’s voluntary departure period elapsed.
 
*3 Thus, in view of the fact that (1) the Immigration Judge has discretionary authority to establish time limits for the DHS’s reply to the respondent’s motion to reopen, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1)(iv); (2) the Immigration Judge allowed the DHS 10 days within which to reply to the respondent’s first motion to reopen as well as to the respondent’s second motion to reopen; and (3) the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s first motion to reopen 12 days after it was filed on March 29, 2006, and denied the respondent’s second motion on June 1, 2006, 22 days after it was filed, there is no basis upon which to presume that the motion would have been adjudicated before the expiration of the respondent’s voluntary departure period even had it been properly filed before March 10, 2006. See Chedad v. Gonzales, 497 F.3d 57, 64 (1st Cir. 2007) (“The relevant provisions of the act simply do not contemplate suspending the voluntary departure period so that aliens who have chosen that form of relief also can pursue reopening.”); see also Matter of Shaar, 21 I. & N. Dec. 541 (BIA 1996), aff’d, 141 F.3d 953 (9th Cir. 1998).
 
In conclusion, we reemphasize the importance of providing a detailed statement in support of the respondent’s ineffectiveness of counsel claim describing “the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard.” See Matter of Rivera, supra. In this case, the evidence submitted for the purpose of establishing the claimed ineffectiveness neither describes the terms according to which the work was to be performed nor how the former counsel’s performance of the work was ineffective. Hence, the respondent has failed to meet his burden of proving that reopening on the basis of his former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness is warranted. See section 240(c)(4) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4) (providing that an alien applying for relief from removal has the burden of establishing that he meets all applicable eligibility requirement for relief and that he merits a favorable act of discretion); accord 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). Thus, we need not reach the respondent’s equitable tolling argument. The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4335838 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 4335861 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DONALD ANTHONY SINGH A.K.A. SINGH, DONALD A.
File: AXXX XX3 848 - Houston, TX
September 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael A. Leppala
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) º - Convicted of aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago and lawful permanent resident of the United States, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 4, 2008, decision ordering him removed as convicted of an aggravated felony on the basis of his Texas conviction for “delivery by actual transfer of cocaine less than 1 gram.” The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that his conviction under section 481.112 of the Texas Health and Safety Code does not constitute an aggravated felony because the statute of conviction is divisible and he argues that his record of conviction does not demonstrate that he was convicted of a “drug trafficking crime.” (Respondent’s Brief at 14.) The respondent also contends that he was denied due process because he alleges that the Immigration Judge did not “scrupulously and conscientiously probe into, inquire of, and explore for all the relevant facts,” his Texas conviction resulted from ineffective assistance of counsel, his conviction is not an aggravated felony as a matter of law, and he should have been permitted to file an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a) (Respondent’s Brief at 3-5). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) contends that the Immigration Judge properly found the respondent to be convicted of an aggravated felony and thus ineligible for discretionary relief because the transfer of cocaine is punishable as a felony under the Controlled Substances Act (“CSA”). See 21 U.S.C. §§ 802(8), (11), (44), 812(c), 841(a), (b)(1)(C).
 
As an initial matter, we reject the respondent’s due process claims because we find that his hearing was “fundamentally fair.” See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of Toro, 17 I&N Dec. 340 (BIA 1980). In particular, the Immigration Judge clearly explained the charges filed by DHS and provided the respondent with opportunities to obtain counsel and file an application for relief (Tr. at 9, 15-17, 22). In addition, reliance on the uncontested evidence of the respondent’s Texas conviction does not violate due process as it is well-settled that neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board has the authority to look beyond a record of conviction to determine the guilt or innocence of an alien in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Reyes, 20 I&N Dec. 789 (BIA 1994); Matter of Fortis, 14 I&N Dec. 576 (BIA 1974). Moreover, because the Immigration Judge found that the respondent was convicted of an aggravated felony, which conviction renders the respondent ineligible for discretionary relief such as cancellation of removal, it was not improper to allow the respondent only to apply for mandatory forms of relief from removal. See sections 208(b)(2)(B)(i) and 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158(b)(2)(B)(i), 1229b(a)(3). Furthermore, we reject the respondent’s assertion of mere legal error as a basis for his due process claim. See, e.g., Sanchez-Cruz v. INS, 255 F.3d 775, 780 (9th Cir. 2001) (holding that “an allegation of mere legal error does not constitute a colorable due process claim”).
 
*2 We also reject the respondent’s contention that his Texas conviction for “delivery by actual transfer of cocaine less than 1 gram” does not constitute an aggravated felony. Even if we agreed with the respondent that section 481.112 of the Texas Health and Safety Code is divisible under the categorical approach because it punishes “deliver¿yº, or possess¿ionº with intent to deliver a controlled substance,” which encompasses conduct that would constitute only a misdemeanor under federal law, such as simple possession of marihuana or “distributing a small amount of marihuana for no remuneration,” see 21 U.S.C. §§ 841(b)(4), 844(a); but see Matter of Aruna, 24 I&N Dec. 452 (BIA 2008,), the respondent’s judgment of conviction indicates that he was convicted under the “delivers” portion of the Texas statute and that cocaine was the controlled substance at issue, which constitutes a drug trafficking crime because it corresponds to the felony drug offense of distribution of a Schedule II controlled substance under the CSA. See 21 U.S.C. §§ 802(8), (11), (44), 812(c), 841(a), (b)(1)(C); see also United States v. Cormier, 468 F.3d 63, 70 n.3 (1st Cir. 2006); United States v. Durham, 464 F.3d 976, 981 n.7 (9th Cir. 2006); United States v. Hester, 140 F.3d 753, 760-61 (8th Cir. 1998); United States v. Jackson, 55 F.3d 1219, 1226 (6th Cir. 1995); United States v. Washington, 41 F.3d 917, 919-20 (4th Cir. 1994). Finally, we note that the respondent’s contention that he is not a recidivist is not relevant here because the respondent’s aggravated felony charge is based on his conviction for “delivery by actual transfer of cocaine” rather than recidivist possession. Thus, the Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent is removable as convicted of an aggravated felony and is not eligible for discretionary relief.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4335861 (BIA)

2008 WL 4222180 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIKS VALER’ YEVICH PANIN A.K.A. FELIKS PANIN
File: AXXX XX3 022 - Philadelphia, PA
August 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tatiana S. Aristova, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Uzbekistan, has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s June 10, 2008, decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent’s request for oral argument before this Board is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Initially, we will review the procedural history in this case. On November 7, 2002, the respondent was served with a Notice to Appear. On August 28, 2003, the Immigration Judge granted a motion to administratively close proceedings filed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). On August 25, 2005, the Immigration Judge granted the DHS’s motion to recalendar. The order noted that a master calendar hearing was scheduled for January 24, 2006. On January 24, 2006, the respondent’s prior attorney was provided notice of a hearing scheduled on January 31, 2006, On January 31, 2006, the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia removal order against the respondent.
 
More than 2 years later, on April 10, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his in absentia removal order with the Immigration Judge. The respondent claimed that he went to Canada on or about January 27, 2006, and that on January 30, 2006, his mother received a telephone call from his prior attorney advising him of the hearing scheduled for the following day. The respondent’s motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge contains a copy of a complaint to the Pennsylvania bar regarding his prior attorney, a letter to his prior attorney informing him of the complaint, and a statement regarding his motion to reopen. On June 10, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent now appeals that decision to this Board. The respondent’s brief on appeal indicates that he is in Canada.
 
We note that generally, “¿aº motion to reopen or to reconsider shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal … proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). However, the present case constitutes an exception to this general rule because it involves an in absentia removal order. Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(h), an in absentia order may be rescinded by filing a motion to reopen: 1) within 180 days of the issuance of the order if the alien can show that exceptional circumstances excused his failure to appear or 2) “at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the ¿Immigration and Nationalityº Act ¿8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1) or (2)º, or … he or she was in … custody and the failure to appear was through no fault of the alien.” “Exceptional circumstances” are defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(l), as those circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as being in federal custody, serious illness, or death of an immediate relative. Ineffective assistance of counsel has been held to qualify as an “exceptional circumstance” which excuses an alien’s absence and allows reopening of removal proceedings in which an in absentia removal order is issued. See Borges v. Gonzales, 402 F.3d 398, 407-408 (3d Cir. 2005). Here, the respondent claims that he was not notified of the removal hearing in time to attend due to his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. Consequently, the Board has jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen the in absentia removal order. See generally Contreras-Rodriguez v. United States Attorney General, 462 F.3d 1314, 1317 (11th Cir. 2006) (finding that the Board and the Immigration Judge retain jurisdiction to reopen proceedings to address whether an alien received sufficient notice of the in absentia removal hearing).
 
*2 Nevertheless, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion. As set forth above, the respondent is claiming that he did not receive notice of the hearing based on ineffective assistance, which the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, has held may constitute an exceptional circumstance. See Borges v. Gonzales, supra. The respondent did not file his motion to reopen his in absentia removal order within 180 days of the order as required when claiming exceptional circumstances. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Moreover, inasmuch as he admits on appeal that his prior attorney notified his mother of the hearing the night before the hearing and that she informed him of the attorney’s telephone call, the respondent cannot show that the 180-day filing deadline for the motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. Therefore, we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen the in absentia removal order.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4222180 (BIA)

2008 WL 4222176 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAMSHER CHAGHANI
File: AXXX XX1 585 - Dallas, TX
August 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raymond J. Sanders, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Judson J. Davis
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 The procedural posture of this case is as follows. At a sixth master calendar hearing held on April 20, 2004, the respondent, a native and citizen of Pakistan, conceded removability, agreed to voluntarily depart the United States on or before June 20, 2004, and waived his right to appeal. The respondent had been granted five continuances to afford the labor department time to adjudicate his pending labor certification. See Immigration Judge’s written decision issued June 30, 2004.
 
On June 15, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. He sought adjustment of status under former section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1255(i), based on his pending employment based immigrant visa petition (I-140). On January 24, 2005, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.¿FN1º On July 8, 2005, and December 26, 2007, we denied the respondent’s first and second motions filed with this Board as being time and number barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § §1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) (an alien may file one motion to reopen and, with certain exceptions not relevant here, it shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal).
 
On August 4, 2008, the respondent filed his third motion to reopen with this Board. Again, the respondent’s motion will be denied as time and number barred. A final administrative order was issued in this case over three years ago on January 24, 2005. The respondent asks us to reopen these proceedings on our own motion. This Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
First, he asserts that his due process rights were denied when the Immigration Judge failed to inform him of his right to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1255(i). The respondent’s argument is misplaced. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent five continuances so that the could obtain an approved labor certification, a prerequisite to adjustment of status. We observe that this record does not contain a copy of the respondent’s pre-April 30, 2001, labor certification allegedly filed by Prembro, Inc. on April 21, 2001. See Respondent’s Motion at 1.
 
*2 Second, the respondent asserts that these proceedings should be reopened because his waiver of his right to appeal was not knowing and voluntary. Contrary to the respondent’s assertions, he was granted voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), which does not require that an alien waive his right to appeal. See Immigration Judge’s written decision issued June 30, 2004. Insofar as the respondent blames his prior counsel for waiving his right to appeal, he has failed to comply with the requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Also, equitable tolling of the 90-day motions deadline is not warranted here. In a recent unpublished case, the Fifth Circuit observed that an argument for equitable tolling “is in essence an argument that the BIA should have exercised its discretion to reopen the proceeding sua sponte based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling.” See Ben Jie Lin v. Mukasey, 2008 WL 2725317 (5th Cr. July 11, 2008). The respondent has not established exceptional circumstances warranting sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, supra.
 
Finally, the respondent asks that we reopen these proceedings based on the United States Supreme Court’s recent decision in Dada v. Mukasey, 128 S. Ct. 2307 (2008). In that case, the Supreme Court held that “… to safeguard the right to pursue a motion to reopen for voluntary departure recipients, the alien must be permitted to withdraw, unilaterally, a voluntary departure request before expiration of the departure period, without regard to the underlying merits of the motion to reopen. As a result, the alien has the option either to abide by the terms, and receive the agreed-upon benefits, of voluntary departure; or, alternatively, to forego those benefits and remain in the United States to pursue an administrative motion.” Id. at 2319.
 
In this case, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen for 245(i) adjustment with the Immigration Judge within the voluntary departure period. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on two grounds. First, the respondent’s motion was not supported by evidence showing his prima facie eligibility for 245(i) adjustment “if his I-140 application should ever be approved.” See Immigration Judge’s written decision issued June 30, 2004. Second, the respondent was barred from adjustment of status pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, based on his failure to depart as agreed. Because the respondent’s motion to reopen was not denied solely on the 240B(d) bar, Dada does not control this case. To date, regardless of the 240B(d) bar, the respondent has not established his prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status because the respondent has not established he is a grandfathered alien for 245(i) adjustment purposes. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.10. Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion is denied as time and number barred.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

1

The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit dismissed the respondent’s petition for review for lack of jurisdiction on June 3, 2005. See Government’s Opposition to Respondent’s August 21, 2007, motion to reopen, Attachment.

IN RE: SILVIA E. SALINAS FUENTES

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Royal F. Berg, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anastasie M. Senat
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 9, 2007, decision, denying her unopposed Motion to Rescind Removal Order. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for the September 21, 2007, hearing. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent’s attorney of record admits that he failed to properly note or calendar the hearing. He further accepts complete responsibility for the respondent’s failure to appear for the hearing. Moreover, he has filed a bar complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities detailing his ineffective assistance. The respondent has submitted an affidavit on appeal indicating that her attorney failed to properly record the September 21, 2007, hearing in his calendar.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). In the case at bar, the respondent’s attorney has admitted that the respondent failed to appear at the hearing due to his failure to properly record the hearing date. We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and the proceedings will be reopened and remanded for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Edward R. Grant

2008 WL 4222171 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KETIYARA KETTIE MTAWALI
File: AXXX XX 890 - Hartford, CT
August 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ryan Bausch, Esquire
*1 In a decision dated April 9, 2007, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent abandoned her request to file for the relief of cancellation of removal by failing to file an application by the deadline of March 9, 2007, and the respondent was ordered removed from the United States to Malawi. The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen on April 24, 2007, which was denied by the Immigration Judge on May 3, 2007. The respondent has appealed both the Immigration Judge’s initial decision dated April 9, 2007, and the Immigration Judge’s subsequent decision dated May 3, 2007, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.¿FN1º The respondent requests acceptance of the first appeal by certification. We will accept the respondent’s first appeal by certification to avoid any question of untimeliness. The appeals will be consolidated and the records will be remanded.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings. She seeks reopening and remand to the Immigration Judge for consideration of newly discovered evidence. The respondent contends that her former attorney, Abdul Abdurahman, failed to file her application for cancellation of removal with the Immigration Court, although she completed the cancellation of removal application with Mr. Abdurahman on March 7, 2007, and she provided him with the application filing fee in January 2007. The respondent has submitted numerous documents in support of her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and in support of her application for cancellation of removal. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Furthermore, her current attorney of record has provided an affidavit concerning his own responsibility in the mishandling of the respondent’s case. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
In light of the respondent’s new allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel concerning her former attorney, Mr. Abdurahman, and the affidavit submitted by her current attorney regarding his responsibility in the mishandling of the respondent’s case, we find that the record must be remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s claim that she failed to timely file her application for cancellation of removal due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). Accordingly, the appeals will be consolidated, and the records will be remanded.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeals are consolidated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The records are remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD

The record reflects that the respondent filed a motion to reconsider with the Hartford Immigration Court on June 1, 2007. However, as an appeal had already been filed by the respondent, the motion was forwarded to the Board of Immigration Appeals.

2008 WL 4222243 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BEATRICE AGUILAR DE NIEVES A.K.A. BEATRIZ AGUILAR-CAMACHO
File: AXXX XX5 357 - Detroit, MI
August 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Arturo Sandoval, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Jebson
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 1, 2007, decision. In that decision the Immigration Judge, inter alia, denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), as a matter of discretion. The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent to be statutoriJy ineligible for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of facts by an Immigration Judge, including those as to the credibility of testimony, to determine whether the findings are “clearly erroneous.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).¿FN1º The Board may review all questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues on appeal from decisions of the Immigration Judge “de novo.” 8 C.F.R. §1003.1(d)(3)(iii).
 
We first note that, while the respondent, in her Notice of Appeal (EOIR-26), initially raises a possible claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we will not consider this issue, the respondent having neither addressed the issue in her Brief, nor met any of the requirements we laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to support such a claim. Turning to the underlying issues, the respondent, who did not dispute any factual findings made by the Immigration Judge, asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her application as a matter of discretion. She asserts that, as she entered the United States in 1983 as a
 
After a determination is made that an alien is removable, and the alien seeks relief from removal which may be granted as a matter of discretion, the Immigration Judge is required to review the record as a whole. See Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581, 585 (BIA 1978). In weighing all of the favorable and adverse factors of record to determine whether cancellation should be granted as a matter of discretion, evidence of a respondent’s bad character or undesirability as a permanent resident of this country may be reviewed with appropriate weight, if any, given to such evidence. See Matter of Sotelo, 23 I&N Dec. 201 (BIA 2001); Matter of Marin, supra. The Immigration Judge met this requirement, considering all of those favorable factors, but nonetheless concluded that the serious adverse factors of record, including the respondent’s criminal activity, outweighed those favorable factors. In weighing the various factors, the Immigration Judge noted that, with respect to two shoplifting convictions, the respondent had demonstrated a complete lack of candor and forthrightness. Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that, while in both her application for cancellation as well as in her testimony on direct examination, she attempted to blame a shoplifting incident on her children, it became clear on cross examination that there were actually two shoplifting incidents, both of which were not only planned by the respondent, but in which she involved her young children. The Immigration Judge also found her immigration violations to be significant, incidents of smuggling aliens into the United States, in 2003 and again in 2004. The Immigration Judge properly considered these serious adverse factors in determining that a favorable exercise of discretion would not be in the best interest of the United States in this instance. We have similarly considered and weighed all of the favorable and adverse factors of record, and agree with the Immigration Judge that relief was not warranted in the exercise of discretion; we therefore affirm the decision for the reasons stated therein. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998); Matter of Marin, supra.
 
*2 Turning to the issue of the respondent’s eligibility for voluntary departure, in order to demonstrate statutory eligibility for the privilege of voluntary departure, one of the requirements the alien must meet is to show that he or she has been physically present in the United States for a period of at least 1 year immediately preceding the date the notice to appear was served under section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). See section 240B(b)(1)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(A).
 
The respondent in the instant case admitted the several allegations set out in the Notice to Appear, issued on December 1, 2003 (Exh. 1; Tr. at 1). She was charged as an arriving alien, and the third allegation was to the effect that on December 1, 2003, she had used the birth certificate of her United States citizen son to assist an alien to try to enter the United States in violation of law. As the respondent has not established that she had been physically present in the United States for a period of at least 1 year immediately preceding the date the notice to appear was issued, she is statutorUy ineligible for voluntary departure. See generally Matter of Romalez, 23 I&N Dec. 423 (BIA 2002); Matter of Arguellas, 22 I&N Dec. 811 (BIA 1999).
 
The following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

This regulation applies to a Notice of Appeal filed after September 25, 2002. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(f); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). permanent resident, is the sole source of support for her six United States citizen children, ranging in age from 6 to 21, and has a stable work history, she merits cancellation of removal as a matter of discretion.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS MANUEL HERNANDEZ-PAYANO A.K.A. BILOMAR GARCIA-PERALTA
File: AXXX XX9 743 - Guaynabo, PR
August 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosaura Gonzalez Rucci, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rossana Rolon Grau
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen; reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision was dismissed in an order dated May 16, 2008. The respondent filed a motion to “reconsider” on June 13, 2008, which we also construe to be a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision, and it shall be supported by pertinent authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). A motion to reconsider must assert that, at the time of our previous decision, an error was made. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
Since the respondent has not presented specific alleged errors of law or fact on the part of the Board in its prior order, the motion fails as one for reconsideration.
 
The respondent, through current counsel, argues that former counsel rendered ineffective assistance of counsel. It is maintained that former counsel erred in admitting the respondent’s removability and asserting that he had no available relief from removal, when he “could have requested adjustment of status and a waiver of ¿hisº conviction.”
 
Although the motion to reopen technically is timely, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c), it is not explained why the respondent waited almost 2 years to complain about the quality of representation of former counsel before the Immigration Judge. Present counsel has been representing the respondent since November 13, 2006, the day the appeal was filed, and no mention of any alleged ineffective assistance was lodged on the appeal form or in a multi-page appellate brief filed by present counsel on October 15, 2007. The first allegation of ineffective assistance comes, curiously, only after a final order was rendered in this case.
 
Be that as it may, the respondent has failed to show that the outcome of this case would have been any different, had former counsel’s assistance been any different. An alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel in proceedings must show that the proceedings were so fundamentally unfair that he or she was prevented from reasonably presenting his or her case. See, e.g., Wang v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 265 (1st Cir. 2004); Saakian v. INS, 252 F.2d 21 (1st Cir. 2001).
 
The record supported the conclusion that the respondent is removable as charged, and the respondent has submitted nothing with his motion to demonstrate otherwise. Further, the respondent has not shown that he is eligible for adjustment of status, a visa petition filed in his favor having been denied by a notice dated October 13, 2006, on the basis that he had engaged in a fraudulent marriage. Consequently, notwithstanding the respondent’s attempt to satisfy the requirements of our holding in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in the absence of actual prejudice, there is no basis for reopening.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied, as is the request for a stay.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 4146709 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED BEKHIT
File: AXXX XX8 782 - Memphis, TN
August 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John S. Richbourg, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (withdrawn)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
 
  
APPLICATION: Review of joint petition to remove conditional status
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.¿FN1º We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s October 26, 2006, decision finding that the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) sustained its burden of establishing that the information in the respondent’s Form I-751 was not true and that the respondent had entered into a marriage for the purpose of evading the immigration laws. See section 216(c)(3)(D) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(3)(D); 8 C.F.R. § 1216.4(d)(2); see also Matter of Mendes, 20 I&N Dec. 833, 835 (BIA 1994); Matter of Lemhammad, 20 I&N Dec. 316 (BIA 1991). See also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(h) (stating that the Board reviews questions of law de novo); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). Nor do we find clear error in the factual findings leading up to that determination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (mandating that the Board “will accept the determination of factual issues by an immigration judge … unless the determination is clearly erroneous”). Notwithstanding the respondent’s assertions on appeal relating to the reliability of the information contained in the Notice of Termination and its supporting notes, the reliability of such documents was not objected to by respondent’s counsel when it was offered as evidence, although he clearly had an opportunity to do so (Tr. at 57; Exhs. 5, 6). Because we do not have a proper claim of ineffective assistance of counsel before us, we therefore find that the respondent is bound by his attorney’s failure to object to the admission of Exhibits 5 and 6. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986).
 
Moreover, the regulations specifically permit the Immigration Judge to receive in evidence any relevant and material, oral or written, statement previously made by the respondent or any other person during any investigation, examination, hearing, or trial. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.7. Introduction of the District Director’s Notice of Termination is, of course, also appropriate as the Immigration Judge, pursuant to section 216(c)(3)(D) of the Act, was required to “review” such determination. Furthermore, there has been no showing of specific disagreement with the accuracy of the interview as related in Exhibit 5, such as might necessitate an opportunity to cross-examine the interviewer. See, e.g., Baliza v. INS, 709 F.2d 1231 (9th Cir. 1983) (recognizing cross-examination not required where accuracy of affidavit not disputed); Marroquin-Manriquez v. INS, 699 F.2d 129 (3d Cir. 1983), cert. denied, 467 U.S. 1259 (1984) (recognizing that a respondent’s right to cross-examine witnesses is not absolute; it is circumscribed by the due process concept of reasonableness). The respondent raised no persuasive challenge to the authenticity or accuracy of Exhibits 5 and 6 as record of the interview. Thus, we conclude that admission and use of the notice was not fundamentally unfair. See, e.g., Baliza v. INS, supra;; Matter of Toro, 17 I&N Dec. 340 (BIA 1980); Matter of Ramirez- Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of Lam, 141& N Dec. 168 (BIA 1972). Once admitted, the information contained in Exhibits 5 and 6 relating to the interview of the respondent and his spouse was clearly relevant and material. Significantly, as the Immigration Judge noted both the Notice of Termination and the respondent’s testimony reveal the existence of an intimate relationship with the mother of the respondent’s children at the time of the respondent’s initial involvement with and during the respondent’s involvement with his former spouse. We also reject the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge impermissibly focused on events that occurred after his marriage. Indeed, although the focus of this inquiry is on the intent of the parties at the time they entered into the marriage, see Matter of McKee, 17 I&N Dec. 332 (BIA 1980), the conduct of the parties after the marriage is relevant to their intent at the time of the marriage. See Lutwak v. United States, 344 U.S. 604 (1953). On this record, and on the facts as found by the Immigration Judge, we decline to set aside the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the DHS sustained its burden of establishing that the information in the I-751 was not true and that the respondent had entered into a marriage for the purpose of evading the immigration laws.
 
*2 Similarly, we find that the respondent’s assertion on appeal that the Immigration Judge improperly imposed his views about marriage onto the respondent is without merit. Under the circumstances, the Immigration Judge properly questioned the authenticity of the respondent’s marriage. Overall, the record reveals that the respondent received a full and fair hearing on the merits of his claim. He was represented by counsel and afforded the opportunity to present, examine, and object to evidence that affected his claim. Although the respondent appears to allege error, he has not on appeal specified such error or articulated or identified prejudice stemming from such errors. More importantly, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that the outcome of his case would have been different had the hearing been conducted in any other manner.
 
Finally, the respondent appeals the denial of voluntary departure. However, the Immigration Judge denied relief as a matter of discretion for the same reasons he relied upon in finding that the respondent had entered into a marriage for the purpose of evading the immigration laws, and we find no reason to disturb this determination. See Matter of Lemhammad, supra at 326.
 
On the whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2008).
