** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NELSON EDGARDO REYES-HERRERA A.K.A. NELSON EDGARDO REYES-HERRERA
File: AXXX XX4 942 - Lancaster, CA
August 15, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James T. Jewett, II
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Removability; waiver of inadmissibility
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the May 7, 2008, decision of an Immigration Judge finding him removable as charged and denying his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, argues that he was afforded ineffective assistance of counsel because his counsel failed to object to the admission of police reports that differed dramatically from his recollection of events surrounding his two convictions. He also argues that the Immigration Judge failed to consider all of the relevant factors of record in concluding that he did not merit a favorable exercise of discretion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues that he was afforded ineffective assistance of counsel because his counsel failed to object to the admission of police reports that contained dramatically different descriptions of the events when compared to his testimony. We do not find that the admission of the police reports serves as a basis for finding ineffective assistance of counsel inasmuch as the record fails to indicate a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. Here, the respondent has failed to offer any evidence in support of his claim. We observe that the Immigration Judge did not rely on the police reports to establish the validity of the respondent’s convictions. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41 (admissibility of evidence to establish criminal convictions). Furthermore, we find no basis on which the police reports could have been prevented from being considered for purposes of discretion. The police reports serve as a business record that is probative on the issue of the respondent’s veracity because the description of events contained therein differed dramatically from the description provided by the respondent.
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent does not merit a favorable exercise of discretion for cancellation of removal. We determined in Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998), that the general considerations for the exercise of discretion outlined in Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581 (BIA 1978), were “equally appropriate in considering requests for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act.” Id. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge “must balance the adverse factors evidencing the alien’s undesirability as a permanent resident with the social and humane considerations presented … to determine whether the granting of … relief appears in the best interest of this country.” Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. at 584-585. Favorable considerations include such factors as family ties within the United States, residence of long duration in this country, evidence of potential hardship to the applicant or the applicant’s family if relief is not granted, service in the United States armed forces, employment history, business or property ties, evidence of service to the community, proof of rehabilitation if a criminal record exists, and other evidence attesting to good character. See Matter of C-V-T-, supra, at 11; Matter of Marin, supra, at 584-585. Among the factors deemed adverse to the alien are: the seriousness and the nature and circumstances of the removal ground; the presence of additional significant violations of this country’s immigration laws; the existence of a criminal record and its nature, recency, and seriousness; and the presence of other evidence indicative of a respondent’s bad character. Id.
 
*2 With respect to the issue of rehabilitation, a respondent who has a record of criminal misconduct will ordinarily be required to establish rehabilitation before relief will be granted as a matter of discretion. Matter of C-V-T-, supra, at 12, citing Matter of Marin, supra, at 588. However, applications are considered on a case-by-case basis, and rehabilitation is but one factor considered. See Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 195-196 (BIA 1990).
 
In this case, we find that the Immigration Judge properly balanced the social and humane considerations of this case against the adverse factors evidencing the respondent’s undesirability as a permanent resident. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent, who is 35 years of age, has resided in the United States since 1999. He has several family members living in the United States, including a United States citizen child, who lives with the child’s grandparents. The respondent has limited employment in the United States, no property, and no assets. His wife is a native and citizen of Nicaragua, living in the United States without legal status.
 
The record also establishes that the Immigration Judge considered the adverse factors of record, including the respondent’s criminal history. The record establishes that the respondent was convicted in the Superior Court of California, County of Los Angeles, on January 17, 2006, for the offense of Grand Theft: Money/Labor/Property in violation of California Penal Code 487(a). The respondent was also convicted on October 17, 2006, in the Superior Court of California, County of San Luis Obispo, for the offense of Receiving Property Known to be Stolen in violation of section 496(a) of the California Penal Code.
 
The respondent has failed to establish that he is worthy of a favorable exercise of discretion as it is not in the best interests of the United States for the respondent to remain in this nation. In particular, the recency of the respondent criminal convictions are a very serious negative factor. The respondent’s repeated criminal misconduct reveals a disregard for the laws of this country. Moreover, the recency of the respondent’s convictions precludes finding that he has demonstrated rehabilitation. The respondent’s criminal history represents a very serious negative factor which the respondent’s equities, specifically family ties and length of residence, do not overcome. In considering the respondent’s family ties, the Immigration Judge correctly weighed as a discretionary matter the degree of hardship that would result if the respondent was removed from the United States. In sum, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly considered all of the relevant evidence relating to the respondent’s equities and the adverse factors of record. The Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act is affirmed based upon the reasons contained in the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4065976 (BIA)

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 204(c) (amended and replaced)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (dismissed)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated (replaced)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(l)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(l)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 204(c)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º -In the United States in violation of law
 
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated December 7, 2007. The respondent contends that his former representative afforded him ineffective assistance of counsel as he failed to contest the merits of the respondent’s denied I-715 petition. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) argues that the respondent’s motion fails because the prior attorney made a strategy choice that does not amount to ineffective assistance. Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998).
 
A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). An alien must also show that the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case and that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that alien is prejudiced if attorney’s conduct may have affected the outcome of the proceeding); Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, supra. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction under which this case arises, has found, however, that aliens need not strictly adhere to the requirements of Matter of Lozada, especially when there is a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” See, e.g., Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
*2 Even if we were to find that the respondent sufficiently complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, we would nonetheless deny the respondent’s motion inasmuch as the respondent has not established prejudice. The respondent has not provided any evidence with the motion which indicates that the outcome of his appeal might have been different had the merits of the I-751 petition been argued. Moreover, the respondent failed to identify substantive arguments that he would have raised before the Immigration Judge regarding the bona fides of his marital relationship. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that he was prejudiced by his former attorney in these proceedings, and reopening is not warranted.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4066002 (BIA)

2008 WL 4066013 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HECTOR PINEDA
File: AXXX XX1 609 - Buffalo, NY
August 8, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Prince Uche Nwakanma, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise Hochul
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on June 30, 1999, because he failed to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on September 6, 2007, but the Immigration Judge denied it on October 23, 2007. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel and exceptional circumstances therefore prevented him from appearing for the hearing. He indicates that his former counsel, Claude Kleefield, failed to advise him of his hearing or that he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. The respondent indicates that he learned in 2002 of what had transpired. The respondent further indicates that he attempted to hire attorney Richard Kassel and then Jesus Marcias to seek reopening on his behalf. He asserts that those individuals did not promptly do so. The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge incorrectly determined that he did not seek reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel with due diligence.
 
The Board has the authority to equitably toll the 180-day period to seek reopening if an alien has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel and seeks reopening based on such with due diligence after becoming aware of the misconduct. See Rashid v. Mukasey, - F.3d -, 2008 WL 2745362 (2nd Cir. July 16, 2008) (stressing an alien’s obligation to seek reopening with due diligence). The respondent was aware in 2002 that he was perhaps the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. It was thus incumbent on him to ensure that his motion to reopen was filed with due diligence. He appears to have waited approximately five years to do so. Such does not constitute due diligence. See Rashid v. Mukasey, supra. The respondent infers that Mr. Kassel and Mr. Marcias may have also been negligent in causing the delay in the filing of the motion. He has not, however, formally accused those individuals of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133 (2nd Cir. 2007) (an alien must substantially comply with the procedural requirements for proving ineffective assistance of counsel). We thus are deprived of an opportunity to consider their rendition of the facts. The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4066013 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 4066014 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARLINDO GOMES
File: AXXX XX7 215-Hartford, CT
August 8, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Manuel Nieves, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on February 19, 2008, at which time we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and to rescind his in absentia deportation order issued on October 15, 1992. The case has now been remanded to us by a May 8, 2008, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit. The respondent’s motion will once more be denied.
 
An order of deportation issued in absentia can be rescinded if the respondent files a motion to reopen (i) within 180 days of the order’s issuance and demonstrates that the failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances,” or (ii) at any time and demonstrates that he did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with former section 242B(a)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(a)(2). See former section 242B(c)(3) of the Act.¿FN1º The respondent’s motion sought rescission based upon the claim that ineffective assistance on the part of respondent’s former counsel was the cause for his failure to attend his October 15, 1992, hearing. Citing our decisions in Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998), and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998), we rejected his argument on the ground that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory time limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation based upon exceptional circumstances.
 
The Second Circuit Court of Appeals has remanded the case with instructions that we reexamine that determination in light of Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595 (2d Cir. 2008), issued the day after our February 19, 2008, order, and Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). In Aris, the court ruled: “We now join our sister circuits in concluding that … a lawyer’s inaccurate advice to his client concerning an immigration hearing date can constitute ‘exceptional circumstances’ excusing the alien’s failure to appear at a deportation hearing … and meriting the reopening of an in absentia deportation order.” Aris v. Mukasey, supra, at 599 (citations omitted). Citing Iavorski v. INS, supra, at 134, the court added in a footnote: “The deadline for moving to reopen may be equitably tolled until the ineffective assistance of counsel ‘is, or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.”’ See Aris v. Mukasey, supra at 599 n.7. In light of this decision by the Second Circuit Court of Appeals, we vacate that portion of our February 19, 2008, order that denied the respondent’s motion on the ground that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory time limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation based upon exceptional circumstances.¿FN2º
 
*2 We will again deny the respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen, for “no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.” Celtic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006) (citing Iavorski v. INS, supra, at 129). The court explained:
In a situation where ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from having the opportunity to present his case for relief, the filing deadline for motions to reopen will be equitably tolled until the ineffective assistance “is, or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.” From the point at which the ineffective assistance of counsel should have been, or was in fact, discovered, “an alien must demonstrate … that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll.” Furthermore, the parties seeking such tolling bear the burden of proving that they were in fact diligent.
 
Id. at 171 (quoting Iavorski v. INS, supra, at 134, 135). The respondent’s motion does not meet that burden.
 
The respondent’s affidavit, submitted in support of his Second Motion to Reopen, states only that (1) his first attorney twice met with him after his first hearing and told the respondent that “he would take care of everything” and get him a “green card;” (2) this attorney failed to inform the respondent of the hearing at which he was ordered deported in absentia; (3) after learning of his attorney’s death, the respondent went to his office where he was told he would have to secure new counsel; (4) he “then got another lawyer” whose “first name was Frank;” (5) he met with and was billed by this attorney on several occasions; (6) the attorney told him he could get the respondent a “green card;” (7) he went to this attorney’s office “three weeks later,” only to learn that he too had died; (8) the respondent “didn’t know what to do” but he did consult “another lawyer” who told the respondent that if he had been granted a “green card,” as “Frank” had said, the respondent “would still be on record as having a green card;” and (9) this information made the respondent feel better and so he thereafter told “people” that he had a “green card.” See Second Motion to Reopen at Exh. A-1. The respondent’s affidavit provides no specific information as to the timing of these various events, an omission fatal to his burden of proof in light of the nearly 15 years that passed between the issuance of the in absentia order and the filing of his first motion. Nor does the affidavit provide the identities of the attorneys he claims to have consulted. Finally, the affidavit fails to state whether the respondent made enquiries of the third attorney or of the government, at any point over the years, about how to get a replacement “green card,” an effort that would be natural in light of what he claims he had been told and which would have revealed to the respondent the misinformation and prior ineffective assistance he had received.
 
*3 We therefore find that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he exercised due diligence, throughout the period he seeks to toll, in discovering and seeking redress from the ineffective assistance of counsel that he has alleged. See Cekic v. INS, supra. Accordingly, we find no basis for equitable tolling of the 180-day statutory filing period for a motion seeking rescission of the respondent’s in absentia deportation order based upon a claim that exceptional circumstances are to blame for his failure to attend his missed hearing. See Aris v. Mukasey, supra at 599 n.7; section 242B(a)(2)of the Act.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Contrary to the assertion in the respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen filed with the Board on January 9, 2008, a motion to reopen deportation proceedings is governed by §242B(c) of the Act, where service of the notice of a hearing at which the respondent failed to appear occurred after June 13, 1992, but prior to April 1, 1997. See Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644, 646 (BIA 1993). The notice of hearing in this case was issued July 16, 1992 (Exh. 3).

2

We also vacate that portion of our February 19, 2008, order stating that we are without jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s motion. See Aris v. Mukasey, supra, at 599 n.6.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE CESAR FERNANDEZ-ZARATE
File: AXXX XX1 067 - Los Angeles, CA
August 7, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Susan E. Hill, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lily C. Hsu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was previously before us on December 21, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 30, 2007. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal entered on March 22, 2006, based on alleged “exceptional circumstances”on account of which the respondent claimed he failed to appear at the hearing held on March 22, 2006. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (2008). The respondent presently moves the Board to reconsider our initial decision pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). However, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Our decision dated December 21, 2007, was based in part upon the respondent’s failure to comply with the procedural prerequisites for a motion to reopen under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Specifically, the respondent failed to support his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with any evidence showing that the individual accused of having provided ineffective legal assistance has been informed of the allegations against him. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592. 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (recognizing that purpose of the notice requirement is to allow prior counsel an opportunity to contest allegations of ineffectiveness so that the adjudicator can accurately assess the claim). In an effort to cure this defect, the respondent has included a declaration from the attorney who allegedly prepared and mailed the complaint to the individual accused of having provided ineffective legal assistance with the instant motion to reconsider.
 
However, the purpose of a motion to reopen is to state new facts to be proven at a prospective hearing date based on previously unavailable or undiscoverable evidence that indicates a meritorious claim for relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); Iturribarria v. IXS, 321 F.3d 889, 895 (9th Cir. 2003). A motion to reconsider, on the other hand, is intended to serve the separate purpose of providing the respondent with a procedural mechanism for “specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). That is, a motion to reopen provides a means for the introduction of new. relevant, and previously unavailable evidence into the record of proceeding, whereas a motion to reconsider is essentially just a request for further review of the record of proceeding in light of the factual and legal errors raised in the motion. Although the respondent has stylized the motion as a motion to reconsider, it is in substance a motion to reopen seeking consideration of new evidence, i.e., the declaration from the attorney who allegedly prepared and mailed the complaint to the individual accused of having provided ineffective legal assistance. Inasmuch, therefore, as the motion to reconsider fails to specify any errors of fact or law in our decision dated December 21, 2007, the motion to reconsider is denied on this basis. If we were to construe the motion as a motion to reopen, the motion would be denied on the grounds that: (1) the motion is numerically barred because the respondent may only file one motion to reopen after the Final administrative decision entered on March 22, 2006; and (2) the evidence offered was previously available to the respondent when he filed the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge on December 4, 2006. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 4065960 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Order Vacated by Jiang v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 11th Cir., May 22, 2009
2008 WL 4066038 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: XUE XIAN JIANG
File: AXXX XX8 468 - Miami, FL
August 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary J. Yerman, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of China, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s April 3, 2007, decision denying her motion to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4) (2008). The appeal is dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent advances two principal arguments. First, the respondent contends that she has established “extraordinary circumstances” warranting equitable tolling of the time and numeric limitations on motions to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal in that she has filed a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against her former attorney (Respondent’s Br. at 5-7). See generally, Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Second, the respondent argues that she has established “changed country conditions arising in the country … to which removal has been ordered,” thereby excepting the motion to reopen from the time and numeric limitations to which such motions are customarily subject (Respondent’s Br. at 7-9). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
A motion to reopen filed in Immigration Court must generally be filed within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and the motion will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that the evidence offered in the motion is material and not previously available or discoverable at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). A motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of removal based on “extraordinary circumstanced” must be filed within 180 days of the entry of the final administrative order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In addition, a motion to reopen filed for the purpose of submitting an application for asylum and related relief may be excepted from the time and numeric limitations if the motion establishes “changed country conditions arising in the country … to which removal has been ordered.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
In this case, the in absentia order of removal was entered against the respondent on September 10, 1999 (I.J. at 1 (April 3, 2007)). The respondent filed her first motion to reopen on January 24, 2000. The Immigration Judge denied that motion, and the Immigration Judge’s decision became a final administrative decision after the appeal period elapsed on February 23, 2000 (I.J. at 1-2 (April 3, 2007)). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39. The respondent subsequently filed a second motion to reopen on May 22, 2006 in which she advanced allegations substantially similar to those presently before us on appeal of ineffective assistance of counsel and of “changed country conditions” in China based on the alleged intensification of the enforcement of China’s coercive population control program. However, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen on June 1, 2006, and, once again, that decision became a final administrative decision after the appeal period elapsed on July 1, 2006 (I.J. at 2 (April 3, 2007)). The respondent does not dispute that the present motion to reopen, filed on January 9, 2007, the third that she has filed, is subject to the time and numeric restrictions on motions to reopen.
 
*2 Turning first to the respondent’s claim that both the time and numeric limitations to which motions to reopen are subject should be tolled on account of the ineffectiveness of her former attorney, we disagree. As the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has recognized, the 180-day filing deadline for motions to reopen an in absentia order of removal is not subject to equitable tolling, under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel or otherwise. See Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999) (“The ¿180-day filing periodº provision is jurisdictional and mandatory.”); see also Abdi v. Attorney General of the United States, 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2005).
 
With respect to the respondent’s remaining claim of “changed country conditions arising in the country … to which removal has been ordered,” we note that the respondent’s claim is principally based on her decision to remain in the United States and give birth to several children after she had been ordered removed (Respondent’s M.T.R. (Exh. A) (Jan. 9, 2007)). However, we do not find that this constitutes a justifiable exception to the regulatory restrictions on motions to reopen in light of China’s coercive population control program. The birth of a second child constitutes changed personal circumstances, not “changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i); see also Zheng v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 869, 872 (8th Cir. 2007); Chen v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 758, 760-61 (7th Cir. 2007); Wang v. BIA, 437 F.3d 270, 273-74 (2d Cir. 2006); Zheng v. United States Department of Justice, 416 F.3d 129, 130 (2d Cir. 2005); Matter of C-W-L-, 24 I&N Dec. 346 (BIA 2007). Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge for the reasons provided in his April 3, 2007, decision that the country conditions evidence submitted by the respondent failed to establish that conditions with respect to China’s coercive population control program have, in fact, changed (I.J. at 3-4 (April 3, 2007)). See generally, Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247 (BIA 2007); Matter of C-C-, 23 I&N Dec. 899 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MR. GERARD RAFOLS SAPADEN A.K.A. GERARD RAFOLS SAPADEN
File: AXXX XX4 603 - Lancaster, CA
August 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Keli M. Reynolds, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on November 23, 1999, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge June 28, 1999, decision finding him removable as an aggravated felon. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen on June 24, 2008. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the pending motion, which will be granted upon our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board will exercise its sua sponte authority over an untimely motion only in an “exceptional situation”); Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002); Ekimian v. INS, 303 F.3d 1153, 1156-60 (9th Cir. 2002) (acknowledging Board’s authority to reopen sua sponte is within its unfettered discretion).
 
The respondent argues that subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s November 23, 1999, decision, finding the respondent removable as charged, the conviction underlying the charge of removal was modified by the Superior Court of West District Judicial District, County of Los Angeles, California, pursuant to a writ of error coram nobis alleging ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º The respondent further argues that because the Court re-sentenced him to 364 days imprisonment, the conviction no longer meets the definition of an aggravated felony theft under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act.
 
In order to qualify as an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, the sentence imposed for a theft offense must be for imprisonment for at least one year. The new sentence imposed for the respondent’s 1998 theft conviction is now less than one year. While it is unclear from the record why the criminal court modified the respondent’s sentence, the Board has determined in Matter of Cota-Vargas, 23 I&N Dec. 849 (BIA 2005), that a trial court’s decision to modify or reduce an alien’s criminal sentence nunc pro tunc is entitled to full faith and credit by the Immigration Judges and the Board of Immigration Appeals, and such a modified or reduced sentence is recognized as valid for purposes of the immigration law without regard to the trial court’s reasons for effecting the modification or reduction. Matter of Song, 23 I&N Dec. 173 (BIA 2001), clarified; Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), distinguished.
 
Consequently, the record will be remanded to enable the Immigration Judge to consider the modification of the respondent’s sentence for his 1998 California theft conviction in light of our decision in Matter of Cota-Vargas, supra, as it affects his removability for an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, and to ascertain whether there presently remain grounds for removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant

IN RE: JORGE VASQUEZ-MAGANA
File: AXXX XX9 363 - San Francisco, CA
August 4, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander H. Lubarsky, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 ORDER: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
 
Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. On December 8, 1995, the Immigration Judge denied his application for a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), because he failed to pay the filing fee as ordered. A document submitted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) shows that the respondent was deported on July 20, 2006. On September 26, 2006, he then submitted a proposed joint motion to reopen with the DHS and filed a courtesy copy of the same motion with the Immigration Court (I.J. at 1). The Immigration Judge mistakenly construed the courtesy copy as a joint motion to reopen, and ordered the proceedings reopened sua sponte on October 11, 2006 (I.J. at 1). On October 13, 2006, the respondent’s counsel filed a statement clarifying that the proposed motion was merely a courtesy copy for the Immigration Court (I.J. at 2). In turn, on January 3, 2007, the DHS filed a motion to vacate the October 11, 2006, order, asserting its lack of consent to reopening and notifying the Immigration Judge for the first time of the respondent’s July 20, 2006, deportation. Presently before the Board is the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s February 20, 2007, decision vacating the prior order. The appeal is dismissed.
 
A motion to reopen must ordinarily be filed within 90 days after the entry of a final administrative order of deportation, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Thus, the respondent has not filed a timely motion. Moreover, he does not argue that an exception to the time bar applies, claiming instead that the proceedings should be reopened sua sponte because his former attorney provided him ineffective assistance of counsel. We disagree. The respondent displayed a lack of due diligence by failing to raise this claim until after his deportation, over a decade after the purported ineffective representation occurred in 1995. Therefore, he cannot establish exceptional circumstances warranting sua sponte reopening. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (motions to reopen are especially disfavored in immigration proceedings because every delay works to the advantage of the deportable alien); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE GONZALO MORETA-VERDEZOTO

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Virginia Carstens, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated July 24, 2007, dismissing the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which found him removable and denied his application for adjustment of status. The respondent’s motion seeks to reopen for adjustment of status (based on an approved visa petition on his behalf by his United States citizen daughter) because of the alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel (Kevin Dehghani). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be granted because the respondent meets the requirements to allege ineffective assistance of counsel, shows prejudice, and shows due diligence so that equitable tolling of the time limitation on motions to reopen is warranted.
 
The facts and pertinent history of the case are as follows. The Immigration Judge issued his decision on January 27, 2006. The respondent appealed. The Board issued its decision on July 24, 2007. The respondent filed the present motion on March 19, 2008.
 
We find that the respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA. 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of Mr. Dehghani. The respondent presents his affidavit;, present counsel’s letter to Mr. Dehghani, which gave him an opportunity to respond, and a complaint filed with the statewide bar counsel in East Hartford, Connecticut.
 
The Board held in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561 (BIA 2003), that even if an alien meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, he or she must still show sufficient prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions. We find that the respondent shows sufficient prejudice. The respondent presents in support of his motion voluminous documentation to show the bona fides of his second marriage, which was at issue at the January 27, 2006, hearing. The respondent, through Mr. Dehghani, presented very little evidence regarding the marriage bona fides. Clearly the evidence now presented may have changed the outcome of the case.
 
The respondent also shows prejudice as regards the ground of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii) ¿falsely claiming United States citizenshipº. The respondent admitted using a false Puerto Rican identification card (Exh. 6), which stated that he was a United States citizen, to obtain a job. The respondent testified about this at the January 27, 2006, hearing, but the date the misrepresentation occurred was not brought up. See Tr. at 22-23, 26, 42-43. The respondent states in his affidavit that the misrepresentation occurred in 1994.¿FN1º This ground of inadmissibility (added by IIRIRA) only applies to representations made on or after September 30, 1996. The respondent thus makes a prima facie showing that the timing of the misrepresentation may have a bearing on the outcome of the case.
 
*2 We find that the respondent exercised due diligence in filing his motion to reopen. The Board issued its decision on July 24, 2007. The respondent met with and retained present counsel on August 13, 2007. The motion to reopen was filed on March 19, 2008, Ninety days from August 13, 2007 (when the respondent discovered that Mr. Dehghani provided ineffective assistance), was November 13, 2007. The period the respondent is asking us to toll runs from November 13, 2007, until March 19, 2008, which is 4 months and 1 week. We find that it is appropriate to equitably toll this time period. The respondent retained present counsel less than 3 weeks after the issuance of our July 24, 2007, decision. Although 4 months and I week is over the 90-day time limit for filing a motion to reopen, it is not excessively so given the actions which needed to be taken, including complying with the Lozada requirements and locating the documents the respondent presents which support the bona fides of his second marriage. See Jin Bo Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 159 (2d Cir. 2006) (finding that under the facts in the case, the motions period could be tolled for 5 months).
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

The card states that it was issued on October 10, 1990, and would be valid only for 2 years from its date of issuance.

2008 WL 4066028 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERT OVUORYE EMOJEVWE
File: AXXX XX1 786 - Oakdale, LA
August 1, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jerry A. Beatmann
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was last before this Board on April 16, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 17, 2008, decision. In our decision, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). On June 19, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider and reopen our decision. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider. The respondent filed a response to the DHS’s opposition.¿FN1º The motion to reopen and reconsider will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider a decision must be filed with the Board within 30 days after the mailing of our decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The record indicates that on May 9, 2008, the respondent requested that we reissue our April 16, 2008, decision. The record also indicates that the respondent attempted to file a motion to reissue our decision on June 9, 2008, but that the motion was rejected for failure to include the required fee or fee waiver request form. On June 19, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and reconsider and requested that we reissue our decision alleging that he had not received a copy of it. The respondent has submitted a copy of an envelope sent by the Office of the Clerk of the Board of Immigration Appeals. The envelope indicates that it was returned to the sender. Inasmuch as it appears that the respondent did not receive a copy of our decision, another copy of our April 16, 2008, decision will be sent to the respondent and we will deem his motion to be timely filed.¿FN2º
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1), a motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority. The respondent claims that we lodged an additional charge of removability against him in our decision. We disagree. Our prior order merely reflects that an additional charge of removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), was lodged against the respondent during proceedings (Exh. 1A). The record indicates that the Immigration Judge did not find him removable on this basis and our order did not disturb that finding (Exh. 1A; Tr. at 20).
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he failed to establish extreme hardship to his United States citizen spouse or children. Our prior decision did not address the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he failed to establish extreme hardship to his wife or children because we found no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not merit relief as a matter of discretion. The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge and this Board erred in looking at his entire indictment, which included counts that were dismissed, to find that the respondent did not merit relief as a matter of discretion. We find no legal error in this examination for purposes of a discretionary determination and no legal error in the determination that the respondent does not merit relief as a matter of discretion.
 
*2 The respondent also requests that we reopen proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion to reopen must state the new facts to be proved and must be supported by evidentiary material. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). We have held that an alien must meet the “heavy burden” of showing that “if proceedings before the immigration judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence offered would likely change the result in the case.” Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 110 (1988) (stating that a motion to reopen is analogous to a motion for a new trial in a criminal case on the basis of newly discovered evidence and that the movant therefore faces a “heavy burden”). We note that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel also requires that the respondent establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his counsel’s ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640.
 
The respondent contends that his prior attorney erred in failing to have him apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229b(b). However, inasmuch as the respondent conceded before the Immigration Judge that he was removable as charged under section 23 7(a)(2)(A)(ii)of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), the respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. See I.J. at 13; section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act (barring aliens convicted of an offense under section 237(a)(2) of the Act from eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act). Furthermore, a copy of a letter from the Alabama State Bar dated June 4, 2008, which the respondent submitted with his motion, provides that two attorneys from the Office of General Counsel of the Alabama State Bar reviewed the respondent’s complaint against his attorney and the attorney’s response. The letter explains that “in view of the nature and content of the complaint and the … response of the attorney¿,º” the Alabama State Bar decided to “take no further action in ¿theº matter.” The respondent also claims that evidence attached to his motion was not investigated by his prior counsel. However, the respondent has not made a prima facie showing that he suffered prejudice as a result of the failure to submit these documents. Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has not made a prima facie showing of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of Coelho, supra. Based on the foregoing, we will deny the respondent’s request for reopening.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: A copy of the Board’s April 16, 2008, decision in this case will be reissued to the respondent.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent contends that we should not consider the DHS’s response to his motion because it was filed more than 10 days after his motion. The regulations provide that opposing parties haver 13 days from the date of service of the motion to file a brief in opposition to a motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The regulations also provide that we may, in our discretion, consider a brief filed out of time. See id. We will do so in the instant case.

2

The DHS’s opposition to the respondent’s motion does not address the respondent’s request for reissuance of our April 16, 2008, decision.

2008 WL 4066046 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR LEWIS ALVAREZ A.K.A. VICTOR ALVAREZ
File: AXXX XX9 357 - El Centro, CA
July 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; removability
 
The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision dated May 22, 2008, in which we summarily dismissed his untimely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s March 19, 2008, decision. The Board will grant the motion to reconsider, vacate our prior order, and exercise jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal by certification. Upon consideration of the merits of the appeal, however, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
In our May 22, 2008, decision we noted that the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on March 19, 2008, the appeal was due at the Board on or before April 18, 2008, and the Notice of Appeal was filed on April 21, 2008. In this motion, the respondent acknowledges that the Notice of Appeal was filed in an untimely manner. He asserts, however, that the delay in filing was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent claims that his former attorney, who reserved appeal on his behalf, was aware of his intention to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision. Furthermore, he claims that he was confident that his attorney would file the appeal because when he attempted to hand her the Notice of Appeal forms, she informed him that she such forms in her office. Finally, he claims that when he contacted the attorney on April 17, 2008, she informed him that she did not file an appeal on his behalf.
 
To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must establish prejudice as well as substantial compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889, 899-900 (9th Cir. 2003). In this case, we find evidence of substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements. In light of the respondent’s claims, we also find that the respondent reasonably demonstrated to his attorney that he was interested in appealing the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Roe v. Flores-Ortega, 528 U.S. 470(2000). Furthermore, in light of the respondent’s prompt submission of the Notice of Appeal within days of the deadline, we find that but for the attorney’s failure to file the appeal, the respondent would have filed an appeal in a timely manner. Id. In sum, the respondent has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with regard to his former attorney’s handling of the appeal.
 
*2 A motion to reconsider may be based on an allegation of material factual errors in the prior decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). In this case, although the respondent asserted in the Notice of Appeal that the attorney failed to file the appeal on his behalf, we did not consider the issue in reaching our decision to summarily dismiss the appeal. Inasmuch as we did not consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim in reaching our previous decision, we will vacate our prior order and accept jurisdiction over the appeal by certification. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). Upon consideration of the merits of the appeal, however, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
During the removal hearings, the respondent, through counsel, conceded removability and applied for no forms of relief. Instead, he moved to continue the proceedings until after April 14, 2008, to enable him to pursue a collateral attack on the criminal conviction underlying the charges of removability. The Immigration Judge denied the continuance and ordered the respondent removable to Belize.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the crime for which his was convicted is not an aggravated felony, and that he is not removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii).¿FN1º We disagree.
 
The respondent does not dispute that on January 13, 2006, he was convicted in the California Superior Court,County of San Bernardino, for the offense of possession or purchase of cocaine base in violation of section 11351.5 of the California Health and Safety Code. According to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this proceeding arises, a state drug offense qualifies as an aggravated felony for immigration purposes only if it would be punishable as a felony under federal drug laws, or if it contains a trafficking element. See Cazarez-Gutierrez v. Ashcroft, 382 F.3d 905, 910-(9th Cir. 2004). An offense is classified as a felony under the federal drug laws if it is punishable by a term of imprisonment exceeding 1 year See 18 U.S.C. § 3559(a)(5); 21 U.S.C. § 802(44). The respondent’s offense, involving the possession of cocaine base for sale, is equivalent to the federal offense of possession of cocaine base with intent to distribute in violation of 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1). Possession of less than 5 grams of cocaine base with intent to distribute is punishable under federal law by a term of imprisonment of up to 20 years, making it a federal felony. See 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(C). Because the respondent’s offense is punishable as a felony under the federal drug laws, it qualifies as an aggravated felony in the Ninth Circuit. The respondent is, therefore, removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*3 The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s May 22, 2008, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent raises no argument contesting the Immigration Judge’s finding that he is also removable as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation under section 23 7(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act.

2008 WL 3919090 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REYNA VENERANDA ANDINO-VALENTIN
File: AXXX XX1 673 - Detroit, MI
July 30, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea Ferrara, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Goulding
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on September 15, 1999. She filed a motion to reopen on September 12, 2007, but the Immigration Judge denied it on November 13, 2007. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she filed a timely motion to reopen. We disagree. The respondent does not dispute that she received the Notice of Hearing. She indicates in her affidavit that she appeared slightly late for her hearing and hired an attorney to seek reopening of these proceedings. She indicates that she learned on March 29, 2005, that her motion to reopen was not filed. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was required to have filed a motion to reopen based ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board would then perhaps have had an opportunity to consider the rendition of the facts as recalled by the respondent’s former counsel. We will not presume that the respondent’s former counsel was negligent in the absence of proof that the respondent complied with the procedural requirements for proving such. See Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 725 (6th Cir. 2007). We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to seek reopening with due diligence. See Dunlap v. United States, 250 F.3d 1001, 1008 (6th Cir. 2001). The respondent was aware on March 29, 2005, that her former counsel had allegedly failed to seek reopening of these proceedings. It was thus incumbent on her to seek reopening with due diligence. Her failure to do so for more than two years is indicative of her lack of diligence exercised in this matter. Reopening is inappropriate.
 
The respondent further argues that these proceedings should be reopened based on changed conditions or circumstances in Honduras. See Matter of A-N- & R-M-N-, 22 I&N Dec. 953 (BIA 1999). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to establish a reasonable likelihood that she would prevail at a hearing based on such. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 3919047 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KURT ELEXANDER BUDD A.K.A. KURT BUDD
File: AXXX XX6 129 - Los Angeles, CA
July 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert West, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated March 16, 2001. In that decision we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision concluding he was removable as charged and ineligible for any relief from removal, including cancellation of removal and a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C §1182(c). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.¿FN1º
 
As an initial matter, we address the question of our jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion. The respondent was deported from the United States on or about March 5, 2003, and illegally reentered on or about August 1, 2003. See Respondent’s Motion at 3.¿FN2º The respondent filed the present motion on February 5, 2008. We have jurisdiction pursuant to Reynoso-Cisneros v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1001 (holding that because the alien departed the United States after the completion of immigration proceedings, the Board had jurisdiction over the alien’s motion to reopen); see also Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979, 982 (9th Cir. 2007).¿FN3º
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent requests equitable tolling based on his claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel (Richard Fraade).¿FN4º See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that the court recognizes equitable tolling of deadlines and numerical limits on motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when an alien is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and upheld in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We conclude that equitable tolling is not warranted.
 
We find that the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural prerequisites for alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent presents a copy of the bar complaint filed against Mr. Fraade which included an affidavit swearing to the contents of his complaint. Even if the affidavit can be construed as a separate declaration, there is no evidence that the respondent has notified Mr. Fraade of the allegations against him before the Board, and likewise no proof that he has been given an opportunity to respond to the allegations made against him to the Board. The respondent asserts that he has submitted emails to Mr. Fraade in this regard, but no such evidence has been submitted with the motion. See Respondent’s Motion at 6. Further, this is not a case where patent ineffective assistance has been shown which would excuse insufficient compliance with Lozada. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002). Therefore, based on insufficient compliance with Lozada, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not considered by the Board, and shall not be considered as a basis to toll the filing deadline for his motion. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 Assuming arguendo substantial compliance with Lozada, we conclude that the respondent does not show ineffective assistance of Mr. Fraade, and thus, does not show prejudice. See Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042, 1046 (9th Cir. 2000) (stating that to show prejudice, the alien only need show plausible grounds for relief). The respondent claims that his former counsel failed to aggressively argue against the removal charge and to present a claim for cancellation of removal.
 
However, under the law as developed at the time of the Immigration Judge’s and Board’s decisions, the respondent’s conviction under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11360(a) rendered him removable as charged under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º, to wit, an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B), 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B).¿FN5º See United States v. Lara-Aceves, 183 F.3d 1007 (9th Cir. 1999), United States v. Estrada-Torres, 179 F.3d 776 (9th Cir. 1999), and United States v. Lomas, 30 F.3d 1191 (9th Cir. 1994). These cases were collectively overruled on April 18, 2001, by United v. Rivera-Sanchez, 247 F.3d 905 (9th Cir. 2001), superseded by statute on other grounds (holding that § 11360(a) was facially too broad to be considered an aggravated felony in a Sentencing Guidelines context). Thus, that decision was issued after the final administrative order of removal was issued here. The respondent incorrectly asserts in his motion that “the Ninth Circuit had ruled on this issue prior to ¿hisº removal hearing.”. See Respondent’s Motion at 7. Thus, based on the applicable law as developed at the time the Board issued its March 16, 2001, decision, the respondent’s conviction qualified as an aggravated felony, which also rendered him statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. 1229b(a)(3).¿FN6º As a result, his prior counsel’s assistance was not ineffective by failing to aggressively argue against the removal charge and for cancellation of removal.
 
We also find that the respondent does not show due diligence. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 897 (stating that the alien must act with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). As noted above, United States v. Rivera-Sanchez was issued shortly after the Board’s dismissal of his appeal. The respondent does not explain the 7-year delay until February of 2008, to raise his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, e.g., based on failure to file a motion based on the change in law. Thus, the respondent has not shown due diligence in bringing his ineffective assistance claim.
 
*3 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied as untimely, and the respondent has not established a basis for equitable tolling of the filing deadline.
 
The respondent argues in the alternative that the Board should exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999) (fundamental change in law may warrant sua sponte reopening notwithstanding otherwise applicable time and number limitations on motions); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (Board will exercise its sua sponte authority over an untimely motion only in an “exceptional situation”). The Board declines to sua sponte reopen these proceedings.
 
The respondent’s arguments in favor of sua sponte reopening are not clear, as he cites to the “Corona Rivera decision” with no citation, as a fundamental change in law. See Respondent’s “Reply to ICE Opposition.” We surmise that the respondent intended to cite to United States v. Rivera-Sanchez, supra. The change in law represented by Rivera-Sanchez predates the respondent’s motion by nearly 7 years. The respondent has not shown due diligence by promptly seeking reopening based on that change in law. Consequently, the interest of finality in immigration proceedings takes precedence. See, e.g., Matter of G-C-L-, 23 I&N Dec. 359 (BIA 2002) (setting forth reasons for no longer sua sponte reopening cases where more than 5 years had passed from a fundamental change in law). Further, the respondent has offered no evidence establishing that his conviction was not for an aggravated felony. Although the burden is on the DHS to establish an alien’s removal under section 237 of the Act, as an alien seeking sua sponte reopening, we find it appropriate to place the burden on the respondent to submit evidence establishing that his conviction involved conduct which would not qualify as an aggravated felony. The respondent has not submitted such evidence. Thus, as an exceptional situation has not been shown, the Board finds insufficient cause to exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent submitted with his motion a copy of a court document purportedly dismissing an indictment against him for illegal reentry after deportation. The basis for the dismissal claims that there may have been “a possible legal problem with the defendant’s removal proceedings.” Contrary to the respondent’s assertions in his motion, he has submitted no evidence that any court has found a legal error in his removal proceedings.

2

The respondent appears to state that he was removed a second time, and perhaps reentered again subsequently; however, the record is not clear and the DHS has not submitted any evidence in this regard. See Respondent’s Motion at 4.

It is noted that the DHS repeated the same incorrect information in its opposition. The single removal charge was under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act (Exh. 1).

2008 WL 3919045 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEXTER LANCELOT RODNEY A.K.A. RODNEY DEXTER A.K.A. DEXTER RODNOY
File: AXX XX7 175 - York, PA
July 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandra Greene, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maureen C. Gaffney
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
This case was last before us on March 6, 2008, when we vacated the Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision and granted the respondent’s motion to remand, in which he asserted that his criminal trial attorney rendered ineffective assistance of counsel in conceding his removability before the Immigration Judge on November 6, 2007.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) now appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2008, decision on remand granting the respondent’s motion to terminate the aggravated felony ground of removal pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii). The DHS contends that it has met its burden of establishing that the respondent’s bank fraud conviction involved a loss to a victim or victims exceeding $ 10,000.00, and therefore is an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(M) of the Act. The respondent, a native and citizen of Guyana, moves for affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision.¿FN1º The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge was correct in the April 11, 2008, decision, which found that the evidence of record fell short of the “clear and convincing standard” set forth in section 240(c)(3)(A) to establish the amount of loss resulting from his federal fraud conviction pursuant to 8 U.S.C. § 1344, as opposed to the theft conviction pursuant to 8 U.S.C. § 641. The Immigration Judge also found that the record did not reveal how the trial judge arrived at the $23,450.21 restitution total, as reflected in the criminal judgment. The DHS’s appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded for a new decision consistent with our findings below.
 
We find that the evidence submitted by the DHS supports a finding that the “loss to the victim” and the restitution amount in this case are attributable to the respondent’s conviction under Count I (Bank Fraud - 18 U.S.C. § 1344). See Nijhawan v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 523 F.3d 387, 399 (3d Cir. 2008)¿FN2º (it is well within the competence of a court to examine the record for clear and convincing evidence of loss caused by the conduct of conviction); see Matter of Babaisakov, 24 I&N Dec. 306 (BIA 2007). The original January 10, 2003, complaint was filed against the respondent by a Postal Inspector. It charged that the combined total of 42 fraudulent checks deposited in the Fleet Bank account opened by the respondent was approximately $33,425.36. The criminal docket for the respondent’s case in the United States District Court for the Southern District of New York, dated February 11, 2004, reflects that a jury found the respondent guilty of Counts 1 and 2. The respondent’s pre-sentence investigation report (PSI), submitted on his May 14, 2004, sentencing date, confirms that the jury found the respondent guilty of Count 1, Bank Fraud under 18 U.S.C. § 1344, and Count 2, Theft of Government Property under 18 U.S.C. § 641. The PSI reveals that the defendant fraudulently deposited stolen checks totaling $37,080.21 into his Fleet Bank account (PSI at 5, item 21).
 
*2 The PSI further shows that as a result of the fraudulent checks that cleared, Fleet Bank, the victim of this offense, suffered losses of $23,450.21 (PSI at 4, item 15). The PSI recommends that full restitution to the victim is required under 18 U.S.C. § 3663A and 18 U.S.C. § 3664, and that restitution in the amount of $23,450.21 is owed to Fleet Bank (PSI at 10, item 59). The trial judge ordered that the respondent pay restitution in the amount recommended in the PSI. We find that this document clearly reveals how the trial judge arrived at the $23,450.21 restitution total, and that it alleges a specific monetary loss related to the respondent’s fraud conviction.
 
We conclude that, taken together, the complaint filed against the respondent, the criminal docket documenting the judgment of conviction, and the PSI with the restitution order, establish that the requisite loss was tied to the respondent’s conviction of bank fraud. See Nijhawan v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 395. We also conclude that a conviction under 18 U.S.C. § 1344 is clearly a “fraud” offense. Id. at 390-91. As Fleet Bank was the “victim” of this fraud offense, and the amount of its loss exceeded $ 10,000.00, we find that the DHS has met its burden of establishing that the respondent was convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43(M).
 
Finally, even though the respondent was found guilty by a jury and not by a plea agreement, we disagree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the PSI did not reflect that the respondent agreed to the alleged loss, or that he was given an opportunity to object to the amount. In Nijhawan, as in the case, the respondent was found guilty of fraud by a jury. In its decision, the Third Circuit held that the language of section 101(a)(43)(M)(i) of the Act does not require a jury to have determined that there was a loss in excess of $ 10,000.00. Nijhawan v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 391. The Third Circuit further held that “the amount of loss in a restitution order, which by its nature is neither found by a jury nor specifically pled to by a defendant, could be considered.” Id. at 394. Therefore, as applied to this case, the fact that the respondent was found guilty by a jury does not prevent the Immigration Judge’s consideration of the alleged loss set forth in the PSI and relied upon by the criminal trial judge.
 
Accordingly, we find that the Immigration Judge erred in terminating proceedings. The evidence submitted by the DHS establishes that the respondent was convicted of a fraud offense, Fleet Bank was the victim of that loss, and the amount of that loss was in excess of $10,000.00.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal of the Department of Homeland Security is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the heading of the respondent’s appellate brief, received on June 26, 2008, incorrectly indicates that he filed it in support of his appeal of the adverse decision of the Immigration Judge, we will accept it as a brief in opposition to the DHS’s appeal.

2

We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit issued its decision in Nijhawan v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, in May 2008, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2008, decision.
