2008 WL 3919064 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHIN YAN CHOU
File: AXX XX9 912 - San Diego, CA
July 25, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Theodore N. Cox, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on April 6, 2004, at which time we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s January 2, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his deportation proceedings due to (1) the respondent’s failure to show that he did not receive notice of the Immigration Court hearing at which he was ordered deported in absentia; and (2) the respondent’s failure to file a timely motion to reopen for the purpose of applying for asylum. In an order dated March 24, 2008, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit found that we and the Immigration Judge had abused our discretion in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen “because, regardless of whether Chou received proper oral notice of his 1993 hearing, the bar to relief under former 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(e)(1) does not apply to asylum claims.” The Immigration Judge did not rule that the bar to relief after the issuance of an in absentia order of removal applied to new asylum claims. Instead, citing the Board’s ruling in Matter of G-C-L-, 23 I&N Dec. 359 (BIA 2002), the Immigration Judge stated that a motion to reopen based upon the respondent’s claim of persecution under China’s coercive population control policies was “subject to the standard time and number limitations on motions, and to the exceptions thereto, as set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.2(c)(2) and (3).” However, the Immigration Judge simply concluded, without any factual findings or analysis, that the “Respondent has not shown that he is eligible for relief that was not previously available to him.” We therefore will vacate our prior order and remand the record to allow the Immigration Judge to consider that aspect of the respondent’s motion to reopen anew.
 
The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals also ordered us to consider the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º The respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance was not properly before us at the time we rendered our prior order, for it was not previously raised in the motion to reopen denied by the Immigration Judge. The Board’s appellate jurisdiction in deportation cases is limited to review of decisions by an Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(b)(2). In addition, the Board “will not engage in fact finding in the course of deciding appeals.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). Therefore, in an effort to comply with the order issued by the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals, we instruct the Immigration Judge to consider on remand the respondent’s motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 ORDER: Our order dated April 6, 2004, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The court concluded that we had failed to address the respondent’s contention that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, while his case was before us on appeal, the respondent advised us in a January 16, 2004, Amended Brief in Support of Respondent’s Appeal that his motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, submitted as part of his first appellate brief on August 7, 2003, did not apply to the proceedings currently on appeal. Instead, such claim applied to proceedings brought under alien number AXX XXX XXX, which had been terminated by a different Immigration Judge on December 10, 2002, with no appeal of this ruling being filed with the Board. We interpreted such statement in the Amended Brief as an abandonment of the claim of ineffective assistance raised in his first appellate brief. We therefore issued no ruling on the question whether the respondent could raise the ineffective assistance claim in these proceedings.

2008 WL 3919058 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NAVROZ KARIM
File: AXX XX9 984 - Houston, TX
July 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Riddhi Desai, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gerrie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated February 19, 2008. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which denied his motion to reopen which sought to reopen his removal proceedings on the basis of ineffective assistance of former counsel (John Mendoza). The respondent’s motion is both time and number barred. The motion will be denied because the respondent does not show sufficient prejudice, so exercise of our sua sponte authority is not warranted.
 
The facts and pertinent history of the case are as follows. The Notice to Appear (Form I-862) was issued on August 21, 2004. The respondent was first represented by attorney Maribel Roldan. On March 16, 2005, Ms. Roldan moved for a change of venue on the respondent’s behalf and also moved for withdrawal as counsel, and noted the respondent was in agreement with both motions. Ms. Roldan stated that the respondent was eligible for asylum and withholding of removal. The respondent was next represented by attorney John Mendoza. In a June 5, 2006, order the Immigration Judge granted the respondent 120 days voluntary departure and the parties waived appeal. The respondent was next represented by attorney Brian Bates, who filed a motion to reopen on September 6, 2006, which alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel Mendoza and submitted the respondent’s asylum application. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely. The respondent appealed and the Board dismissed the appeal in a February 19, 2008, decision (the respondent was represented on appeal by attorney J. Jesus Rios). The present motion to reopen was filed on May 19, 2008.
 
In the respondent’s present motion he alleges ineffective assistance of former counsels Bates and Rios. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit (in whose jurisdiction the present case arises) does not have to date any published decisions on equitable tolling of the time and number limits for motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The Fifth Circuit has recognized and approved of our decisions in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the requirements to allege ineffective assistance of counsel) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (the alien must also show that former counsel’s actions were prejudicial to his case). See Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 164-65 (5th Cir. 2006) (stating the Board has determined that ineffective assistance of counsel is a valid ground for reopening, in egregious circumstances, and when prejudice is shown). We will thus consider whether the respondent’s motion merits exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”).
 
*2 We find that the respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, to allege ineffective assistance of former counsels Bates and Rios. The respondent has filed a detailed affidavit, present counsel’s letters to Mr. Bates and Mr. Rios, the response of Mr. Bates, and two bar complaints.
 
The Board held in Matter of Assaad, supra, at 561, that even if an alien meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, he or she must still show sufficient prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions. We find that the respondent does not show sufficient prejudice. The respondent claims that the ineffective assistance of former counsels Mendoza, Bates, and Rios combined to prevent him from timely filing for asylum and withholding of removal. The respondent, however, has never alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel Roldan. Ms. Roldan mentioned that the respondent was eligible for asylum and withholding of removal in the respondent’s motion for change of venue and her motion for withdrawal and noted the respondent was in agreement with both motions. The respondent was thus aware of the availability of applying for asylum and withholding of removal on or before March 16, 2005 (when the motion was filed), and cannot rely on the claimed ineffective assistance of succeeding former counsels for his failure to timely apply for asylum and withholding. We thus decline to exercise our sua sponte authority. See Matter of G-D-, supra.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 3919058 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 3919061 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FENG YU DONG
File: AXX XX4 291 - Chicago, IL
July 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sam Chung, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lynn K. Hollander
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of the People’s Republic of China. She was ordered removed from the United States on October 2, 2003, because she failed to appear for her hearing. Notice of that hearing was mailed to her former counsel, Liu Yu. The record also contains letters from former counsel dated June 19, 2003 and April 27, 2007. Therein, counsel indicates that the respondent did not stay in contact with her. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on March 28, 2005. The Immigration Judge denied the request on May 16, 2005, and that decision was not appealed. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen on May 3, 2007. The Immigration Judge denied the request on June 13, 2007, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
There is no time limit to request reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia based on lack of notice. However, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) provides that an alien may file only one motion to seek reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia. Consequently, the respondent’s second motion to reopen is numerically-barred from consideration. We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was adequately notified of the hearing even if the second motion was not barred from consideration. Notice of hearing sent to an alien’s attorney is a proper method for notifying an alien of a hearing. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985). We will not sua sponte find that the respondent’s former attorney was unauthorized to represent her in these proceedings absent the presentation of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim that satisfies the procedural requirements. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 3919061 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 3919105 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHRISTOPHER ANDRE GAYLE A.K.A. MIKE RICHARDSON
File: AXX XX4 430 - San Antonio, TX
July 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cori White
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on May 30, 2008, when it affirmed the Immigration Judge’s February 26, 2008, decision denying the respondent’s application for protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT). The respondent has filed a timely motion for “reconsideration,”¿FN1º and the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition. The motion will be denied. The request for a waiver of filing fees is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1), 1003.8.
 
In its May 2008 decision, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal because he had failed to show that he was actually prejudiced by his former counsel’s failure to successfully challenge a change in venue in his removal proceedings from San Pedro, California, to San Antonio, Texas. The Board also concluded that the respondent had not met the procedural requirements for establishing that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383 (5th Cir. 2001); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
With his motion, the respondent has submitted documentation that he filed a complaint against his former counsel with the State Bar of California in April 2008 (which concluded in May 2008 that the matter did not warrant action). The respondent also submitted a copy of a March 2008 letter he received from his former counsel, although this document had already been submitted to and considered by the Board prior to its May 2008 decision. It is still questionable whether the respondent has detailed the agreement he entered into with his former counsel, although this is alluded to somewhat in the text of his motion (Motion at 3-4).
 
In any case, the respondent has still failed to show how he was actually prejudiced by the alleged failure of his former counsel to contest the change of venue from San Pedro to San Antonio. Although the venue change conceivably could have made it more difficult for witnesses favorable to the respondent to testify in person, he has not shown that the venue change prevented these individuals from testifying by telephone or from submitting affidavits or letters in his support. Indeed, he has not submitted (with his motion) any affidavits or letters from these individuals confirming what their testimony would have been.
 
The respondent also argues in his motion that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to allow the respondent’s former counsel to withdraw from representing the respondent following the venue change (Motion at 3). However, the respondent still has not shown how he was actually prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s actions (i.e., how the withdrawal of his former counsel and the appointment of a new counsel would have potentially altered the outcome of these proceedings). See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 3919069 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR MANUEL MONTANO A.K.A. VICTOR MANUEL MONTANO DOMINGUEZ
File: AXX XX8 402 - Napanoch, NY
July 24, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura A. Michalec
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopen
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before this Board on August 3, 2007, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent ineligible for relief from removal and ordering the respondent removed from the United States. On June 16, 2008, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks to reopen these proceedings based upon an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Alternatively, the respondent requests that this Board sua sponte reopen his case.
 
The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 3, 2007. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before November 1, 2007. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until June 16, 2008. The motion is therefore untimely.
 
The respondent contends that he was forced to represent himself before the Immigration Judge as his family was not provided enough time to secure the funds to hire an attorney which amounts to an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. We find no merit to this claim. First, the respondent cannot succeed in proving an ineffective assistance of counsel claim since he did not retain the services of an attorney but represented himself. The respondent’s claim on appeal is not a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel but a denial of a right to counsel argument. Second, the respondent should have raised this issue on appeal not in the instant motion. Notwithstanding, during the hearing on December 20, 2006, the respondent acknowledged that he received the list of free legal providers and his case was continued in order for the respondent to retain the services of an attorney (Tr. at 1-4). On January 24, 2007, the respondent appeared without an attorney and requested a continuance in order to retain an attorney. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent a second continuance to retain the services of an attorney. The Immigration Judge warned the respondent that this was his final adjournment and if he did not retain the services of an attorney at the next hearing he would have to represent himself (Tr. at 5-6). On March 21, 2007, the respondent appeared without an attorney and the Immigration Judge informed the respondent that he would have to represent himself as previously advised. We find that the Immigration Judge correctly advised the respondent of free legal services and the respondent acknowledged that he did in fact receive the list of free legal services as required by the regulations. See Picca v. Mukasey, 512 F.3d 75 (2nd Cir. 2007) (regulation specifically states that in a removal proceeding, the Immigration Judge shall advise the respondent of the availability of free legal services and ascertain that the respondent has received a list of such programs.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(a) (emphasis added). This language makes plain that it is the IJ who must advise immigrants of the availability of free legal services during the removal proceeding. See Montilla v. INS, 926 F.2d at 166 (noting that the regulations then in effect, which were substantively identical to those at issue here, required that immigrants “be given a list of available free legal services at the deportation hearing” (emphasis added)). Therefore, we find that the respondent was not denied his right to counsel. We also decline to sua sponte reopen the respondent’s case. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied as untimely filed. The request for a stay is denied.
 
*2 John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 3919069 (BIA)
End of Document

APPLICATIONS: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on September 28, 2005, when we denied the respondent’s motion for reconsideration and reopening of her removal proceedings. On reconsideration, we initially note that the July 14, 2005, Board decision accepted the respondent’s appeal under the provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). Having done so, the Board acquired jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion to remand to apply for adjustment of status. The motion to remand was accompanied, inter alia, by an approved immediate relative visa petition that had been filed on the respondent’s behalf by her United States citizen husband. This was new evidence and, once the Board accepted jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal, the motion reflected prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. Accordingly, on further consideration, we grant the respondent’s motion to remand to provide her the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status before the Immigration Judge. The issue regarding the claim of ineffective assistance of prior counsel is now moot.
 
ORDER: The Board’s order dated September 28, 2005, is hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: On reconsideration, the Board’s order dated July 14, 2005, is amended and the respondent’s motion to remand is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.

2008 WL 3919078 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DILCIA TORRES-ESCOBAR
File: AXX XX3 322 - Newark, NJ
July 23, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Moses Apsan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Court. The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 31, 2007, denial of his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of deportation entered on May 23, 2007. His motion to reopen was filed with the Immigration Court on May 31, 2007, within a week of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order, alleging a lack of notice of the scheduled hearing after the motion to re-calendar the case was granted. On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Court requested in writing that the respondent file representations in connection with his motion, and he asserts that he provided a written response to the Immigration Court on June 8, 2007. He further asserts that, in this submission, counsel acknowledged receiving notice and argued that the respondent’s failure to appear was due to counsel’s error.
 
A review of the record, however, reveals no written request for submissions from the Immigration Court. Nor does it contain the written response supplementing the motion, which the respondent claims to have submitted. It appears, therefore, that the record may be incomplete. Furthermore, the claimed contents of the written submission would appear to have shifted the claim from one of lack of notice to one of failure to appear due to “exceptional circumstances,” i.e. his attorney’s ineffective assistance of counsel. Section 242B(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8U.S.C. §1252b(c); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). Yet, the Immigration Judge’s July 31, 2007, decision addresses only the issue of notice. Under these circumstances, we find it appropriate to remand for the Immigration Court to ensure the completeness of the record and to further consider the motion and issue a new decision.
 
Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration and adjudication of the motion to reopen and to rescind the in absentia deportation order.
 
Juan P. Osuna
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 3861923 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO RAUL CAMPOS-GRANADOS
File: AXX XX3 454 - Harlingen, TX
July 21, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Mendez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Delia I. Gonzalez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s written decision dated May 31, 2007, which denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his administratively final deportation proceedings. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent cites to precedent decisions of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit in support of his arguments that he received inadequate notice of his deportation proceedings, and that the entry of an in absentia order of deportation therefore violated his due process rights. However, the respondent’s motion arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, and he provides no controlling legal authority in support of the foregoing arguments. See generally Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25 (BIA 1989) (providing that the Board follows circuit precedent in cases arising in that circuit). Thus, despite the respondent’s appellate contentions, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to establish that his failure to appear at his deportation hearing was caused by a lack of notice of such hearing. In particular, the respondent’s claim that he received inadequate notice of his deportation hearing in light of his age is unavailing, as he was over 14 years old when he received his Order to Show Cause. See I.J. (written decision dated May 31, 2007) at 1; Exhs. 1A, 4A. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 242.3 (1989 version) (providing that an Order to Show Cause could not be served on a minor under the age of 14) (emphasis added).
 
Moreover, as the Immigration Judge noted, despite the respondent’s claim that he was “not made aware” of his obligations to appear in Immigration Court, the Order to Show Cause was personally served on the respondent. See I.J. (written decision dated May 31, 2007) at 1-2; Exh. 1A. Furthermore, several documents warning him, in Spanish, of his obligation to keep the Immigration Court and the Immigration and Naturalization Service (the predecessor to the Department of Homeland Security) apprised of his current address were personally served on the respondent. See Exhs. 2B-2C.
 
As the Immigration Judge observed, the hearing notices relating to the respondent’s deportation proceedings were properly addressed to the last address provided by the respondent when he was warned of the address reporting requirements, and the respondent does not claim that the hearing notices failed to be delivered to such location. See I.J. (written decision dated May 31, 2007) at 1-2. Based on the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to establish that he was not provided with proper notice of his deportation hearings. The Immigration Judge therefore correctly declined to reopen the respondent’s deportation proceedings based on the respondent’s claim of inadequate notice.
 
*2 The respondent also contends that he is eligible for relief from deportation for which he was ineligible at the time he was ordered deported. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under former section 242B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b, if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided that there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under former section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b). Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision, and after September 30, 1996. The respondent is consequently precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.¿FN1º Furthermore, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s contentions that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled under Ninth Circuit precedent or that the untimeliness of his motion should be excused due to his ignorance of the filing deadlines. Likewise, although the respondent suggests that the untimeliness of his motion to reopen was the result of ineffective assistance of prior counsel, he has failed to satisfy any of the procedural requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). As the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and the respondent has not demonstrated that his motion is exempt from the general time limitations, we find that the Immigration Judge was correct in denying the respondent’s motion based on the respondent’s alternate request for reopening pursuant to Matter of M-S-, supra. See I.J. (written decision dated May 31, 2007) at 2.
 
Finally, although the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge failed to provide a reasoned analysis for the denial of his motion to reopen as required by Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002), we find that the Immigration Judge’s decision adequately identifies and explains the reasons for denying the respondent’s motion. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We therefore decline to address the issue of prima facie eligibility for relief from deportation, or whether the respondent would deserve a favorable exercise of discretion.

2008 WL 3919107 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AFRIM PRELJVUKAJ
File: AXX XX4 840 - El Paso, TX
July 15, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lynn Olinger, Esquire
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R, § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
On March 26, 1991, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 5, 1990, in absentia decision, and denied the respondent’s motion to remand the record for failure to establish reasonable cause for his failure to appear at the hearing. On April 4, 1997, we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for adjustment of status, and on July 29, 1998, we denied his second motion to reopen proceedings for adjustment of status, asylum, suspension of deportation, and withholding of deportation. The respondent has now filed his third motion to reopen proceedings, wherein he currently requests relief from deportation and argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge and this Board.
 
Initially, we agree with the respondent’s argument that he is unaffected by numerical and timeliness requirements due to the date of his Order to Show Cause (OSC). In our precedent decision Matter of Cruz-Garcia, 22 I&N Dec. 1155 (BIA 1999), we recognized that the former regulation set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 3.23(b)(4)(iii) imposes no time or numerical limitation on aliens seeking to reopen deportation proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 242(b) of the Act. Accordingly, our prior 1998 decision, finding his second motion to reopen barred under 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(c)(2), was incorrect. However, we do note that while the respondent currently argues that the Board erred in that 1998 decision because the respondent presented prima facie evidence that “he never received notice of his 1990 hearing,” we note that his 1998 motion requested a remand to apply for adjustment of status and did not address his 1990 in absentia hearing.
 
Nonetheless, the respondent now requests that he be excepted from these timeliness and numerical limitations because he received ineffective assistance of counsel and, again, because of the application of 8 C.F.R. § 3.23(b)(4)(iii). The respondent argues that his representative had been disbarred 7 years prior to his representation of the respondent, and that he did not, inter alia, properly address the in absentia issue which is postured herein. Moreover, the respondent also appears to argue that his representative’s firm did not properly advise him of the date and time for his hearing. Given our previous error in finding the respondent barred by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), and given the respondent’s current compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), we will grant the respondent’s motion, and remand the record for further proceedings regarding the respondent’s requests for relief. The following orders will, thus, be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 3919107 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUZDI KRKUTI
File: AXX XX4 511 - El Paso, TX
July 14, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael P. DiRaimondo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William M. Hunt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 11, 2008, denying his motion to reopen deportation proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed in part and sustained in part; the record will be remanded.
 
Upon review of the record, we adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge, except with regard to the order of deportation. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 3994). In reaching this conclusion, we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion as untimely, as the motion was filed nearly 24 years after the respondent agreed to a stipulated order of deportation, and well-after the regulatory deadlines for motions to reopen provided under 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), 1003.43. Although the respondent presents sympathetic circumstances, he has neither demonstrated that he qualifies for an exception to the statutory and regulatory filing deadlines nor set forth circumstances that would justify equitable tolling of this lengthy period. He has not detailed a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, nor met the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Further, the respondent has failed to establish diligence. See, e.g., Baldwin County Welcome Center v. Brown, 466 U.S. 147, 151 (1984) (requiring diligence for invocation of equitable tolling); Galayr v. INS, 196 F.3d 1257 (5th Cir. 1999).
 
Given the record before the Immigration Judge, we cannot find that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion in declining to reopen proceedings sua sponte, and we likewise find that the circumstances presented by the respondent do not warrant the exercise of our limited discretion to reopen sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (“the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
The respondent also asserts that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to explore his eligibility for adjustment of status. The fact that the respondent may have become eligible for this relief after the time limitation on motions to reopen had expired does not excuse the untimeliness of his motion. Moreover, even if the respondent’s motion were timely, the respondent was not prima facie eligible for adjustment of status because there was no immigrant visa is immediately available to him. See sections 201(b)(2)(A)(i), 203, 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1151(b)(2)(A)(i), 1153, 1255(a); Matter of Garcia, 16 I&N Dec. 653, 657 (BIA 1978).¿FN1º
 
*2 In addition, the respondent claims that the Immigration Judge erred in holding that the fact that Yugoslavia, the country designated in the 1984 Order of Deportation, no longer exists is a matter for the Department of Homeland Security to resolve (Respondent’s Brief at 5-6). An alien who has been ordered removed may designate one country to which he wants to be removed and the Attorney General shall remove the alien to the country the alien so designates. See, e.g., section 241(b), 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b). Although the respondent was provided with an opportunity to designate Yugoslavia in 1984, given the passage of time and the changes to the country since his designation we find it appropriate to remand the record for the sole purpose of providing the respondent the opportunity to designate a country of removal.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed and the request for a stay of deportation is denied
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for the sole purpose of allowing the respondent the opportunity to designate a country of removal.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent is seeking the opportunity to pursue adjustment of status. Adjustment of status is an extraordinary form of relief that was not designed to supersede the regular consular process of visa issuance abroad, and which may be granted only where a favorable exercise of discretion is shown to be warranted. See Matter of Bias, 15 I&N Dec. 626, 630 (BIA 1974; A.G. 1976); Matter of Arai, 13 I&N Dec. 494 (BIA 1970). Although we decline to express an opinion on the likelihood of the respondent’s success in such efforts, once abroad he may seek relief from the regular consular process, including any applicable waivers.

2008 WL 3861927 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OREST IVANOVICH YAKIMIV
File: AXX XX3 040 - Buffalo, NY
July 10, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ralph A. Horton, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carla J. Hengerer
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 21, 2008, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on December 21, 2007. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that a notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s counsel on July 31, 2007, which constitutes more than adequate time for the respondent to have become apprised of the court date.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that he did not receive written notice of the hearing at his home address, nor did his attorney advise him of the new hearing date. The respondent indicates that he was unaware of the in absentia removal order until he was taken into custody. In support of his motion to reopen, the respondent submitted a declaration and an attorney affirmation from his counsel of record, Ralph A. Horton, Equire. The respondent asserts in his declaration that the Immigration Judge is aware that his representative failed to act accordingly and his ineffectiveness affected him. In an attorney affirmation dated February 14, 2008, Mr. Horton states that he is at a loss to explain how this matter came to the point of both he and the respondent missing the December 27, 2007, hearing date.¿FN1º He further explains that his younger brother died suddenly in October 2007, and consequently, his attention to work suffered and he became substantially behind in attending to clients’ affairs, thereby missing the notice of hearing pertaining to the respondent.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent’s attorney has admitted that he became substantially behind in attending to his clients’ affairs due to a sudden death in his family, and he missed the notice of hearing for the respondent. Moreover, the record reflects that the notice of hearing was sent only to Mr, Horton, and the respondent submitted a declaration stating that he never received notice of the hearing from the Immigration Court or his attorney. We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Alrefae v. Chertoff, 471 F.3d 353 (2d Cir. 2006) (court determined that IJ erred in rejecting alien’s claim of nonreceipt by failing to explain why alien hadn’t rebutted the presumption of receipt; IJ erred by not analyzing alien’s claim of exceptional circumstances independently of his claim of nonreceipt; and IJ failed to provide any explanation for his conclusion that alien failed to establish prima facie eligibility for reopening). We note further that the requirement that a bar complaint be filed is to ensure that collusion does not occur, and that the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is a valid one. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Therefore, as we find that counsel did afford the respondent ineffective assistance and in light of the lack of any bar complaint, we shall forward this matter to the Executive Office for Immigration Review Bar counsel for review and the institution of any disciplinary measures deemed appropriate. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that Mr. Horton incorrectly states the date of the hearing in his attorney affirmation. The hearing date was December 21, 2007.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTIN CALDERON GUTIERREZ A.K.A. MARTIN CALTERON
File: AXX XX4 876 - San Francisco, CA
July 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Micheal K. Mehr, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ozlem B. Barnard, Esquire
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on January 16, 2008, when we dismissed without opinion the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2006. The respondent presently requests that we reconsider our January 16, 2008, decision under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º However, because we conclude that the respondent has failed to (1) establish that the 30-day filing deadline should be equitably tolled and (2) satisfactorily comply with the procedural requirements by which motions to reconsider under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed, the respondent’s motion will be denied. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In addition, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal will be correspondingly denied as moot. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f).
 
Turning first to the equitable tolling issue, the respondent filed the presumptively time-barred motion to reconsider (accompanied by the motion for stay of removal) on March 14, 2008. However, the respondent argues that he meets the legal requirements necessary to equitably toll the 30-day filing deadline by which his otherwise untimely motion to reconsider is barred. See, e.g., Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999) (applying equitable tolling doctrine to 90-day filing period for motions to reopen where alien is prevented by his legal representative’s fraud from filing motion timely). We disagree.
 
In Lopez v. INS, supra., the Ninth Circuit recognized that the 90-day filing deadline applicable to motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the respondent establishes that he has been prevented from meeting the 90-day deadline by “fraudulent legal representation” of which he remains ignorant “without any fault or want of diligence or care on his part.” Lopez v. INS, supra., at 1100 (citation and internal quotation marks omitted). In this case, the respondent contends that his former counsel’s statement in a letter dated January 18, 2008, that our decision dated January 16, 2008, is “final” and that he is “not eligible to appeal” our decision rises to the level of fraudulent legal representation (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. A).
 
We conclude that former counsel’s statements do not constitute fraudulent legal representation because they were correct as a matter of law when they were made. First, our decision dated January 16, 2008, is a final administrative order or removal. See section 242(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(a). Second, the respondent’s former counsel was correct in advising the respondent that he was “not eligible to appeal” our decision or, in actual fact, the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2006, because the Act has expressly foreclosed from judicial review discretionary determinations, such as the one rendered by the Immigration Judge (and affirmed without further opinion by us) denying the respondent’s request for relief from removal under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1994), on discretionary grounds. See section 242(a)(2)(B)(ii), (C) of the Act; Fernandez v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2006). Thus, we conclude that the respondent’s motion for reconsideration is time-barred and not subject to equitable tolling.
 
*2 Even were the respondent’s motion for reconsideration timely filed, the motion also fails to comply with the procedural requirements by which motions to reconsider under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed.¿FN2º In Matter of Lozada, supra, we set forth the elementary procedural requirements for a facially cognizable motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The first among them is the requirement that “the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard.” Id. at 637 (emphasis added).
 
In this case, the respondent’s declaration attached to the motion to reconsider fails to describe with sufficient detail the “agreement that was entered into with counsel.” Specifically, the respondent’s declaration only reflects that the respondent retained his former counsel in October of 2004 to represent him during the removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge, and that she “represented ¿the respondentº in an appeal before the Board of Immigration Appeals (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. B).”¿FN3º Thus, the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel’s representation of him before us extended beyond filing the Notice of Appeal, submitting a brief or written statement in accordance with the established briefing schedule, and apprising the respondent of the issuance of our decision, all of which the respondent’s former counsel evidently did in accordance with their agreement. The respondent, in short, has failed to establish that the agreement he entered into with his former counsel provided for any legal representation after our adjudication of the respondent’s appeal. Further, we note that former counsel’s letter to the respondent dated January 18, 2008, invited the respondent to contact former counsel in the event he wished to make any further inquiries of his case (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. A). The respondent evidently declined the invitation to do so (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. B). Accordingly, we conclude that the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel acted ineffectively.¿FN4º Thus, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In evident acknowledgment of his motion to reconsider’s untimeliness, the respondent simultaneously filed a motion to reopen the record of proceedings on March 14, 2008. However, the purpose of a motion to reopen is to state new facts to be proven at a prospective hearing date based on previously unavailable or undiscoverable evidence that indicates a meritorious claim for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (2008); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 895 (9th Cir. 2003). Here the respondent argues that the intervening decision published by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, Camins v. Gonzales, 500 F.3d 872 (9th Cir. 2007), has rendered our decision dated January 16, 2008, legally erroneous. Thus, the respondent requests reconsideration of our earlier decision, not reopening for the purpose of submitting new, previously unavailable evidence or new applications for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied because the respondent seeks reconsideration of our prior decision in light of an alleged change in the law subsequent to the issuance of the final administrative order of removal in this case. Further, the respondent’s motion to reopen is also defective due to its failure to comply with the basic regulatory requirement that the motion be supported by new, previously unavailable evidence or new applications for relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).

2

We are mindful that the Ninth Circuit has provided for a limited number of circumstances under which it will not respect our strict adherence to the procedural prerequisites for a motion to reopen or reconsider under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 938 (9th Cir. 2003) (excusing alien’s failure to lodge a complaint with the appropriate state disciplinary authorities where alien offered a reasonable excuse for this omission and there was no suggestion of collusion between the alien and former counsel); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002) (dispensing with the Lozada requirements where counsel’s ineffective assistance was obvious and undisputed on the face of the record); Ontiveros-Lopez v. INS, 213 F.3d 1121, 1124-25 (9th Cir. 2000) (concluding that “arbitrary application” of the Lozada requirement is not warranted if alien shows “diligent efforts” to comply were unsuccessful due to factors beyond his control). However, we do not find that any of the circumstances requiring relaxation of the Lozada requirements within the territorial jurisdiction of the Ninth Circuit is present in this case. It is fundamental to establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that the respondent show that his former counsel’s conduct ran afoul of an agreement for specific legal representation, and the respondent has failed to satisfactorily make such a showing. That is, the respondent has not provided sufficient evidence to establish that the filing of either post-decision motions with us after we issued the final administrative order of removal on January 16, 2008, or a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit was within the scope of the respondent’s agreement with his former counsel.

3

We observe, with respect to the insufficiency of the respondent’s evidence in this regard, that the respondent has failed to either (1) support the motion for reconsideration with a copy of the fee agreement(s) he entered into with his former counsel or (2) provide any explanation for his inability to do so.

4

We note in closing that Camins v. Gonzales, supra, was published on August 28, 2007, over six months after the respondent, acting through his former counsel, filed the appellate brief in this case. In the absence of evidence showing that the parameters of the agreement entered into between the respondent and his former counsel included the filing of supplemental briefs, post-decision motions, or a petition for review to the Ninth Circuit, however, we are unable to conclude and unwilling to presume that it did.


2008 WL 3861940 (BIA)

2008 WL 2938473 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON ARCADIO GUTIERREZ CASTILLO
File: AXX XX5 617 - Boston, MA
July 7, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John E. MacDonald, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on April 22, 2008, on a motion to reconsider, at which time we vacated our prior November 30, 2007, order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for ineffective assistance of counsel, and found that while the respondent was technically eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(h) of the Act on a nunc pro tunc basis, the respondent still had failed to demonstrate a sufficient basis to afford the respondent nunc pro tunc relief Accordingly, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our previous denial of his motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider our April 22, 2008, decision, arguing that we erred in considering issues of fact which should have been properly remanded to the Immigration Court. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is barred by regulation. A party may not seek reconsideration of a decision which denied a previous motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). Herein, the respondent requests that we reconsider our previous April 22, 2008, denial of his motion to reconsider our November 30, 2007, decision. However, due to the unique posture of the case, in which we vacated, in part, the November 30, 2007, decision, we will also address the merits of his decision. In short, the respondent argues that the Board erred in our previous decision by finding that he was entitled to consideration of his waiver of inadmissibility on a nunc pro tunc basis. Specifically, we held that, in accordance with Matter of Balao, 20 I&N Dec. 440 (BIA 1992), the respondent had not demonstrated that he had departed and returned to the United States since the time of his 1980 conviction; moreover, we found that his delay in receiving a grant of his Form I-130 petition was not an error justifying nunc pro tunc consideration. See generally Fierro v. Reno, 217 F.3d 1 (1st Cir. 2000). The respondent now argues that he did, indeed, depart the United States subsequent to his 1980 conviction, and he further argues that the delay in the adjudication of his Form I-130 is a finding of fact which should be addressed, in the first instance, by the Immigration Judge.
 
Generally, a motion to reopen will not be granted unless it states new and material facts, is supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material, and establishes a prima facie case of statutory AXX XXX XXX and discretionary eligibility for the underlying substantive relief sought. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992); 8 CF.R. §§ 1003.2 and 1003.8. In the case of claims under the standards set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), an alien must demonstrate at least prima facie evidence that the outcome of the case would have been different if he had been represented by adequate counsel. We find, in the instant case, that the respondent simply did not demonstrate prima facie eligibility for relief so as to demonstrate prejudice in his initial November 30, 2007, ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent now argues that he had no idea that his departure and reentry into the United States was “relevant” until our April 22, 2008, decision; however, it is he who raised the issue of nunc pro tunc availability and should have been aware of the law set forth in Matter of Balao, supra, and Matter of Parodi, 17 I&N Dec. 608 (BIA 1980). Similarly, the respondent did not present a modicum of evidence that error or bad faith existed in the timing of his Form I-130 approval, but argues only that this issue should be explored at the Immigration Court level. Inasmuch as the respondent carries the burden of demonstrating that a motion to reopen is warranted, we find no error in our previous decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. Accordingly, inasmuch as the respondent does not identify specific error in our prior order, or call our attention to a change of law stated or relied upon in our earlier decision, the motion is denied. See 8 CF.R. § 1003.2; see Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 2938454 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OKECHUKU AGWARA UCHE
File: AXX XX9 946 - Los Angeles, CA
July 2, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Audra Behne, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 30, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision of October 1, 2003, denying the respondent’s application for adjustment of status, but granting him 60 days voluntary departure. On December 28, 2007, the respondent filed the instant motion, which is captioned as a motion to reopen but is more properly construed as a motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)-(c). On January 8, 2008, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) timely filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based upon the previous factual record, whereas a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006). In this case, the respondent’s motion is captioned as a motion to reopen. However, the respondent failed to explain how the fact that the total period of time that an alien may be granted voluntary departure, including any extensions, may not exceed 120 days, is new or previously unavailable evidence (Mot. at 4). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(f). Instead, as we will discuss more fully below, the respondent’s motion contests the correctness of the Board’s decision dated August 30, 2007. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is more properly construed as a motion to reconsider.
 
Regardless of how we construe the respondent’s motion, it is untimely. A motion to reconsider must be filed with the Board within 30 days after the mailing of the Board’s prior decision, whereas a motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2), (c)(2). In this case, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before October 1, 2007, and a motion to reopen would have been due on or before November 28, 2007. However, the Board did not receive the respondent’s motion until December 28, 2007. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion was filed out of time, and accordingly, will be denied.
 
Relying on Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001), the respondent argues that the motions filing deadline should be equitably tolled because the Board misinterpreted the amended regulations promulgated on May 12, 2006, pertaining to the eligibility of arriving aliens in removal proceedings to apply for adjustment of status (Mot. at 2-4). We disagree. As we explained in our decision dated August 30, 2007, the amended regulations make clear that an Immigration Judge does not have jurisdiction to adjudicate an application for adjustment of status filed by an arriving alien in removal proceedings except in a very limited circumstance, involving aliens who previously filed adjustment applications, departed and returned to the United States under advance parole, and were subsequently placed in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(ii). Accordingly, we determined that since the respondent is an arriving alien and does not come within the narrow exception which would permit an Immigration Judge to consider his application for adjustment of status, a remand was not warranted in this case. In so doing, we noted that the respondent must pursue any application for adjustment of status with U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) independent of these removal proceedings. See generally Matter of Castro, 21 I&N Dec. 379 (BIA 1996).
 
*2 Similarly, relying on the supplementary information published along with the amended regulations, the respondent argues that the Board failed to consider the respondent’s claim under Matter of Garcia, 16 I&N Dec. 653 (BIA 1978) and Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) (Mot. at 5-7). However, the supplementary information upon which the respondent relies does not carry the same binding authority as the regulation itself. Moreover, the excerpt which the respondent quotes in his motion is drawn from the section seeking public comment on whether rules limiting the exercise of discretion or implementing a presumption against favorably exercising discretion should be established in order to preserve the integrity of the nonimmigrant and immigrant visa issuance process. See 71 Fed. Reg. 27585, 27588-90 (May 12, 2006).
 
The respondent also argues that he has been denied a meaningful opportunity to apply for adjustment of status as an arriving alien (Mot. at 7-9). However, the regulations simply require that the respondent pursue his application for adjustment of status with USCIS independent of these removal proceedings. In addition, it is well-settled that the immigrant visa process remains the proper means for an alien to seek admission to the United States as a lawful permanent resident. See Santos v, INS, 375 F.2d 262, 264 (9th Cir. 1967). Accordingly, the respondent makes no argument in his motion that would persuade us that we misinterpreted the amended regulations such that equitably tolling the motions filing deadline would be warranted in this case.
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks to have this Board exercise its sua sponte authority to grant his motion (Mot. at 5), we note that our sua sponte authority is limited to exceptional circumstances, and we are not persuaded that such circumstances present themselves here. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992).
 
The respondent also appears to seek to have this Board reissue our decision of August 30, 2007. In general, we will reissue a decision where some administrative error at the Board has resulted in a defect of service which, if not corrected, would prevent a party from perfecting a timely appeal or complying with a voluntary departure deadline. In addition, we will generally reissue a decision in order to remedy an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, e.g., where an alien’s attorney received notice of our decision but failed to inform the alien of such receipt until after the relevant filing or voluntary departure deadline has passed. However, neither situation presents itself here. Accordingly, we find no basis upon which to reissue our decision of August 30, 2007. Finally, to the extent that the respondent seeks to have this Board further extend his voluntary departure period, we have no authority to do so. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(f).
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2938454 (BIA)
End of Document
