2008 WL 2783067 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ABRAHAM SANCHEZ-PEREZ
File: AXX XX0 370 - New York, NY
June 11, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Alexis Rojas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 7, 2007. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The facts and pertinent history of the case are as follows. The respondent was personally served with the Order to Show Cause (Form I-221) on July 10, 1995. The Order to Show Cause, including the notice of rights and consequences, was read to the respondent in the Spanish language. At the August 22, 1997, master calendar hearing counsel was personally served with the notice of hearing for November 21, 1997. Neither the respondent nor counsel appeared for the November 21, 1997, hearing and the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order the same day. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court on August 21, 2007. The Immigration Judge denied the motion in a September 7, 2007, decision (Exhs. 1, 2).
 
The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to rescind the in absentia order. The motion was untimely. Section 242B(c)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3) (1995), provides, in pertinent part, that a deportation order issued following in absentia proceedings may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Court within 180 days after the date of the deportation order if the alien demonstrates that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. Even assuming ineffective assistance of counsel as claimed by the respondent and his own error, the result in the case would not change. The respondent and counsel discovered their errors on November 24, 1997, when they appeared at the Immigration Court and were told that the hearing had been held on November 21, 1997, and an in absentia order was issued. The respondent took no further action concerning his deportation case until March of 2005 when he went to counsel’s office. Further, the respondent did not file the motion to reopen until August 21, 2007. The respondent did not show due diligence to warrant equitable tolling of the 180-day time limit. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006) (the court concluded that the aliens had not met their burden of showing why they had not been diligent over a 2-year period).
 
The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status. The motion was untimely. The Board held in Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), that an alien is not required to rescind her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence the alien received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. The respondent’s motion, however, is untimely under the general time limitation for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) and 1003.23(b)(3). Although the respondent received the oral warnings in 1995, more than 5 years have elapsed and the bar under section 242B(e) of the Act no longer applies. Finally, we see no reason why the Immigration Judge should have exercised her sua sponte authority over the respondent’s motion. Becoming eligible for relief after a final order did not constitute an extraordinary circumstance. See generally Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
David W. Crosland
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2783067 (BIA)

2008 WL 2517592 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO VALDIVIA-TOSTADO
File: AXX XX9 212 - Reno, NV
June 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven P. Brazelton, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen and reissue our decision dated March 2, 2006. In our order, we dismissed the appeal of an Immigration Judge order denying a request for a continuance and ordering the respondent removed. The motion is untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent requests reissuance of our decision to allow a petition for review to be timely filed in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The respondent argues that, as former counsel acknowledge they failed to timely file a petition for review at the Ninth Circuit by filing the currently pending petition late, ineffective assistance of counsel has been established warranting reopening. See e.g. Dearinger v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042 (9th Cir. 2000). The motion includes some evidence of compliance with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in that counsel has admitted the late filing in a declaration and also sent a letter to “self-report” the untimely filing to the State Bar of Nevada (see Motion at Exhs. E, F). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
However, the motion does not establish a plausible grounds for relief that would support a finding of substantial prejudice by the former counsel’s incompetence. Jie Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent’s claim is the same as that adjudicated on appeal, i.e., that the Immigration Judge should have granted what would essentially have been an indefinite continuance to wait until the petition (Form I-130) filed on the respondent’s behalf is current. While a continuance may be granted for good cause, as the respondent is not eligible for relief, no good cause had been shown to warrant a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6. The priority date for the respondent’s approved petition is January 25, 1996, and, as a fourth preference family-sponsored visa for an alien from Mexico, that category was not current at the time of our decision, and is still not current.¿FN1º
 
The motion does not establish there was ineffective assistance that substantially prejudiced the respondent during the removal proceedings over which we have jurisdiction. See Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241 (9th Cir. 2000); Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968 (9th Cir. 2004). The motion is untimely and does not establish an exception. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Visa Bulletin for June 2008 indicates that visas are available for this category only for priority dates of December 15, 1994, or earlier.

2008 WL 2517595 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE AGUILAR-LOPEZ
File: AXX XX1 777 - San Francisco, CA
June 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rhoda Wilkinson Domingo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph Q. Lynch
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case has been the subject of multiple prior decisions by this Board. On March 19, 1991, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance, suspension of deportation, and voluntary departure. On February 8, 2005, the Board denied a motion to reopen as untimely filed, finding that equitable tolling on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel was not warranted in the absence of due diligence, a showing of that actual ineffective assistance was provided, or a showing of prejudice and that reopening for consideration of adjustment of status was also not warranted in the absence of an application for the relief sought with supporting documentation. On March 31, 2005, we denied a motion to reconsider our February 8, 2005, decision, finding no error and denied a motion to reopen our March 19, 1991, decision as untimely and number barred. The respondent has reported that he filed a petition for review of our February 8, 2005, decision with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which was dismissed on February 27, 2007, with a mandate issued on April 23, 2007. On February 11, 2008, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen our March 19, 1991, decision, and to stay his removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the pending motion, which is denied.
 
The respondent’s third motion to reopen is untimely (filed after September 30,1996) and number barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent claims that the deadline for filing a motion to reopen should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance provided by Sherry Lesson, the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal and by Robert G. Berke, the attorney whom the respondent alleges represented him in the filing of the first motion to reopen which we denied on February 8, 2005, as well as in motion we denied on March 31, 2005, which was a second motion to reopen and a motion to reconsider the first denial of reopening.
 
As indicated, we have previously considered and denied a motion to reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of Ms. Lesson and denied a motion to reconsider that decision and a second motion to reopen. The respondent in the current motion to reopen essentially maintains that he became aware fo the full extent of Ms. Lesson’s ineffective assistance and of the ineffective assistance of Mr. Berke on January 11, 2008, when his current counsel received a copy of his file. The current motion to reopen based on the alleged ineffectiveness of Ms. Lesson is untimely and number barred, as noted in prior decisions. As set forth in our prior decisions, we find that equitable tolling is not warranted because the respondent did not establish that he acted with due diligence. There is no plausible basis for concluding that an alien could not with reasonable diligence become aware of an attorney’s alleged ineffective assistance, where, as here, the alien previously filed a motion alleging such ineffective assistance years earlier and where the prior motion was denied in part for lack of due diligence.
 
*2 The respondent’s marriage to his wife occurred on January 11, 1999, years after the his appeal was dismissed in 1991. His wife became a United States citizen through naturalization on November 10, 1999.¿FN1º The visa petition filed on his behalf by his wife was approved on September 8, 2000. Consequently, it is not alleged or shown that there was ineffective assistance of Ms. Lesson in the failure to seek adjustment of the respondent’s status. Again, we do not find that ineffective assistance on the part of this attorney has otherwise been shown.
 
Although the respondent now claims ineffective assistance of Mr. Berke in the preparation of the first motion to reopen, the record reveals that Robert Kahn, Esquire, represented the respondent before the Board in the first motion to reopen. See BIA 2/8/05; EOIR-27 filed 12/4/04. Thus, there can have been no ineffective assistance in the filing of that motion on the part of Mr. Berke. In any event, as the respondent still has not shown that equitable tolling of the first untimely motion to reopen was warranted on the basis of ineffective assistance of Ms. Lesson, there could be no ineffective assistance with regard to the attorney who represented him in the filing of the first motion. The respondent also alleges that Mr. Berke provided ineffective assistance in the filing of the second motion because Mr. Berke failed to provide an application for adjustment of status with the motion to reconsider the denial of reopening and the second motion to reopen. However, that motion was not denied due to the absence of an adjustment application. We essentially denied reconsideration due to the absence of a showing of any error of fact or law in the denial of the first motion to reopen. The denial of the first motion was based primarily on the failure to show that equitable tolling of the time limit on reopening was warranted. The failure to submit evidence in support of adjustment of status was a separate and independent basis for the denial of the first motion. We denied the second motion to reopen for consideration of adjustment of status as untimely and number barred. Thus, we do not find that ineffective assistance on the part of Mr. Berke has been shown.
 
The respondent in the current motion to reopen essentially maintains that he became aware of the full extent of Ms. Lesson’s ineffective assistance and of the ineffective assistance of Mr. Berke on January 11, 2008, when his current counsel received a copy of his file. He states that he is eligible for adjustment of status on the basis of a visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen wife which has been approved. He states that his criminal convictions have been expunged and that should a waiver be necessary he is eligible based on hardship to his United States citizen relatives which includes illness of his wife and daughter and his son’s service in the United States Armed Forces. Nevertheless, we decline to reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a), an action taken only in “exceptional situations.” Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002); Toufighi v. Mukasey, 510 F.3d 1059, 1063, fn.8 (9th Cir. 2007) (court lacks jurisdiction to review Board’s decision to not reopen proceedings under its sua sponte authority).
 
*3 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied as untimely filed and number barred. The request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
David W. Crosland
FOR THE BOARD

1

The copy of the naturalization certificate of the respondent’s wife presented with the current motion does not contain a legible date. However, a copy of this certificate presented with a prior motion shows that naturalization occurred on November 10, 1999.

2008 WL 2517580 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEXANDER ESPINOSA-MARIN
File: AXX XX1 724 - El Paso, TX
June 6, 2008
 
IN DEPORTTION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anthony D. Collins, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael Pleters
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of Colombia. He was served personally with an Order to Show Cause on August 24, 1989, and he was placed in deportation proceedings. On September 7, 1989, the respondent’s former attorney, Monty Roberson, entered into a stipulation with the Immigration Judge and the former Immigration and Naturalization Service. The stipulation provided that the respondent understood the nature of the proceedings, and he conceded that he was deportable from the United States based on the allegations contained in the Order to Show Cause, The record also contains proof that the respondent was granted on September 7, 1989, thirty days to depart the United States voluntarily. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on February 15, 2008. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on February 27, 2008, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed after September 30, 1996, and it is therefore untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent indicates on appeal and in his affidavit that he does not recall having been granted voluntary departure by an Immigration Judge on September 7, 1989. He infers that he was not present in Immigration Court when the order was entered, did not know of its existence, and he would not have entered into such an arrangement even had he been present in Immigration Court.
 
The Board has sua sponte authority to reopen a proceedings notwithstanding the timeliness of the request when an exceptional situation is shown. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Here, the respondent implies that his former attorney entered into an agreement for the respondent to leave the United States voluntarily without the respondent’s consent or knowledge. Under these circumstances, it is appropriate for the respondent to have filed an ineffective assistance of counsel allegation against his former attorney. His failure to do so is a factor against granting sua sponte reopening. We further consider that the respondent was aware in 1989 that he was in deportation proceedings. He also indicated in paragraph 13 of his affidavit that he had received letters from immigration authorities, and he was aware that he was subject to deportation. The respondent provides no explanation for why it required so many years for him to verify his immigration status. We do not find under these circumstances that an exceptional situation exists to reopen these proceedings sua sponte even if the respondent was unaware of the actions taken on his behalf by former counsel. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2517580 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2517567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEETA GUZMAN A.K.A. SEETA PRESAUD
File: AXX XX0 128 - New York, NY
June 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nadine A. Brown, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evalyn Douchy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 14, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal. On November 13, 2007, the respondent filed this motion to reopen our decision to enable her to apply for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. As the motion has been filed more than 90 days after our previous decision, it is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reopening based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel against the attorneys who previously represented her in these proceedings. The 90-day deadline imposed on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Jin Bo Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 160 (2d Cir. 2006). In order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, however, an applicant must comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, an applicant seeking to toll the 90-day filing deadline must show that she exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period of time for which she seeks tolling. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). In the instant motion, the respondent has utterly failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements. Furthermore, it is not apparent from the evidence now before us that the respondent exercised due diligence in this matter. In this regard, we note that the respondent has not explained how her former attorneys’ alleged misconduct resulted in the untimeliness of the instant motion, and she has not described any measures that she took leading up to the filing of this untimely motion. We find, therefore, that equitable tolling based on the claims of ineffective assistance of counsel is not warranted in this case.¿FN1º Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We find no evidence of any exceptional situation which would warrant reopening of the respondent’s case on our own motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“As a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”).

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on January 17, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. At that time, we found that the Immigration Judge appropriately denied the respondent’s motion to reopen her in absentia deportation proceedings.
 
On January 14, 2008, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit remanded the respondent’s case to this Board. The Court found that we abused our discretion in upholding the denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen because the record does not contain a signed certified mail return receipt to indicate that the respondent was properly served with the Order to Show Cause (OSC), citing Chaidez v. Gonzales, 486 F.3d 1079, 1087 (9th Cir. 2007). The Court directed the Board to address, if necessary, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that the determination on the respondent’s claims of a lack of proper service of the OSC, and allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel, requires further factual findings. In view of the Court’s order, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge to address these claims.
 
ORDER: The Board’s order dated January 17, 2006, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the order of the court and this opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMPSON OWUSU-BEDIAKO
File: AXX XX0 696 - New York, NY
May 30, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gnoleba Seri, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Virna A. Wright
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to Reopen
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Ghana, has made an untimely motion to reopen proceedings that were administratively final on February 17, 1994. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion, which was received on April 9, 2008, was filed more than 14 years after the Board’s decision in this case and is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent has offered no evidence that he qualifies for an exception to the time limitation on motions to reopen based on changed country conditions or circumstances in Ghana. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1129a(c)(7)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(changed country conditions or changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality).
 
To the extent that the respondent requests that we reopen proceedings on our own motion, we find that the respondent has failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our sua sponte authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen proceedings on its own motion is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). Although the respondent has made a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in an effort to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to timely file this motion, we find that he has failed to establish due diligence. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000)(Second Circuit recognizes equitable tolling of motions deadlines in ineffective assistance of counsel cases where the alien has demonstrated that both that his constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, and that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll). He has failed to adequately explain why he waited 14 years after our last decision before he was able to discover and pursue a remedy for the allegedly ineffective assistance.
 
Moreover, even if the motions deadline was tolled in this case, the motion would be denied for failure to show prejudice resulting from the alleged failures of prior counsel. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&NDec. 553 (BIA2003)(alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must show prejudice). A review of the Immigration Judge’s decision indicates that he weighed the respondent’s equities against the seriousness of his crime. The respondent’s conviction for heroin trafficking was significant, and there is no indication that there were any ameliorating factors that were not made known to the Immigration Judge, or which arguably would have swayed the Immigration Judge’s conclusions in this case. Indeed, the Immigration Judge rendered an adverse credibility finding in this case (I.J. at 5) (“I do not believe that the respondent was telling me the truth concerning his criminal activities in pursuit of the sale of heroin, nor do I believe his account of how the $12,000 in cash found in his residence came to be there”). It is unlikely, therefore, that reopening would change the result in this case.
 
*2 The respondent had a full and fair hearing on his application for section 212(c) relief before the Immigration Judge, and the Board considered all relevant factors before affirming the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of the relief requested. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2400959 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2400974 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: INGA MICHELLE DEWAR
File: AXX XX2 038 - Miami, FL
May 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George E. Crimarco, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated February 13, 2008. The motion is unopposed. Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the applicant setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. In order to succeed in a claim of ineffective assistance of prior counsel, a respondent must also demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result. See Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
The respondent maintains that prior counsel, in preparing the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), failed to (i) inform the respondent of the types of evidence that would be necessary to meet the statutory requirement that she demonstrate 7 years’ continuous residence in the United States after having been admitted in any status, and (ii) direct the respondent to gather evidence, and ensure the appearance of her witnesses to testify, in support of her claim that she merited cancellation of removal as a matter of discretion. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998). However, when the Immigration Judge and respondent’s counsel discussed the possibility that the respondent had not accumulated sufficient evidence to meet her burden of proof, the respondent stated in court that she had not failed to cooperate with counsel through inattention or design but instead because she was embarrassed to visit his office without the means to pay for his services (Tr. at 18-19). The record contains no response from prior counsel as to the respondent’s current claim of ineffective assistance. On the current record, it is not clear to what extent the respondent’s lack of cooperation, and to what extent prior counsel’s failure to prepare the respondent, were responsible for the dearth of evidence presented before the Immigration Judge. We therefore will remand the record with instructions that the Immigration Judge conduct the appropriate fact-finding to determine whether the respondent has met the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada.
 
*2 If, after resolving the necessary factual and legal arguments, the Immigration Judge determines that the respondent’s claims as to ineffective assistance are without merit, the Immigration Judge should once again enter an order of removal. If, however, the Immigration Judge determines that the respondent’s claims as to ineffective assistance have merit, the Immigration Judge should then entertain any applications for relief from removal.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2400974 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: PEDRO ESTRADA-GOMEZ
File: AXX XX4 394 - Salt Lake City, UT
May 28, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
German T. Flores, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Matthew R. Hall
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Suspension of deportation; fair hearing; remand
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely appeal from a decision, dated March 14, 2007, in which an Immigration Judge denied his application for suspension of deportation for failure to satisfy the extreme hardship and good moral character requirements for such relief.¿FN1º See former section 244 (a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254(a)(1). On January 3, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record to the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(4). The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
Initially, we will consider the respondent’s motion to remand. The respondent argues that the record should be remanded because the transcript “is replete with indiscernible statements.” See Respondent’s Motion at 16. While it is true that the transcript of the proceedings includes certain indiscernible lines of testimony, the respondent has not specified how such indiscernible testimony might have affected the outcome of this case. Specifically, he has not identified what material facts, if any, were omitted from the record. We find, therefore, that he has failed to show that such indiscernible lines of testimony caused him prejudice. See Matter of Exame, 18 I&N Dec. 303 (BIA 1982). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to remand will be denied.
 
With regard to the respondent’s appeal, we first find that the transcript of the proceedings is sufficiently clear to judge the merits of the appeal. Second, we find that on appeal the respondent has not contested the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact or identified any factual errors underlying the Immigration Judge’s decision. Third, we find that the Immigration Judge properly applied the appropriate legal standards and correctly concluded that the respondent failed to establish eligibility for suspension of deportation on both good moral character and extreme hardship grounds. In sum, we find no reason to alter the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent also moves for reconsideration of our December 4, 2007, decision denying a previous motion to accept a late-filed appeal brief. The respondent argues that the failure to file the brief in a timely manner was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He has not shown, however, that he suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Hickman v. Spears, 160 F.3d 1269, 1273 (10th Cir. 1998) (explaining that to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, a petitioner must show, inter alia, that the deficient performance was prejudicial). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

IN RE: JORGE RIVERA-HERNANDEZ A.K.A. JORGE RIVERA A.K.A. JOERGE RIVERA
File: AXX XX8 144 - Los Angeles, CA
May 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fatma E. Marouf, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reissuance
 
*1 On January 25, 2008, the respondent filed this motion to reissue our December 14, 2004, dismissal of his appeal from an Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2003, decision. The respondent claims that the office of his former attorney first informed him of our decision on December 9, 2007. See Tab A (Respondent’s Declaration), at 4. The respondent now requests that we reopen and reissue our decision to enable him to file a timely petition for judicial review. We will grant the motion.
 
Pursuant to the regulations, the Board is obligated to give notice of its decisions by serving them, physically or by mail, upon the parties affected. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(f) and 1003.13. According to the record, the Board mailed its decision dismissing the appeal to the respondent’s former attorney. The respondent claims after the appeal was filed, he repeatedly contacted his former attorney to inquire into the status of his case. See Tab at 3. He further claims that he immediately contacted his former attorney’s office after learning on or about December 8, 2007, that he was required to report before December 14, 2007, to the nearest Immigration and Customs Enforcement office. See Tab A at 4; see also Tab P (Notice to Obligor to Deliver Alien). On December 9, 2007, the office of the respondent’s former attorney informed the respondent that his appeal had been dismissed. See Tab A at 4. The respondent then sought the counsel of his current attorney, and he obtained a copy of his file from his former attorney’s office. Id. at 4-5. In the file, the respondent found a letter, addressed to this Board, in which the respondent’s former attorney states that “¿nºeither our office nor the client is in receipt of the decision notice.” Id. at 5. The respondent has proffered a copy of that letter, dated May 22, 2006, and date-stamped by the Board on May 30, 2006, in which the respondent’s former attorney requested a copy of our December 14, 2004, decision. See Tab Q.
 
Given the particular circumstances of this case, we find it appropriate to reissue our decision dismissing the appeal, thereby enabling the respondent to petition for judicial review. First, the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Tabs A and T. See also Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Second, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has explained that when alien is prevented from filing an appeal in immigration proceeding due to counsel’s error, and is thereby deprived of appellate proceeding entirely, error mandates a presumption of prejudice inasmuch as the adversary process itself has been rendered presumptively unreliable. See Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042, 1045 (9th Cir. 2000). In this case, although the Board properly mailed its decision to the respondent’s former attorney, the May 22, 2006, letter confirms that the respondent was not notified of our decision prior to the deadline for filing a petition for judicial review. Furthermore, in his sworn declaration, the respondent has stated that despite repeated inquiries, his former attorney did not inform him of our decision prior to December 9, 2007. See generally Singh v. Gonzales, 494 F.3d 1170, 1173 (9th Cir. 2007) (remanding a case to the Board to consider and specifically address the effect of affidavits of the alien and his attorney). Accordingly, we will reopen the proceedings on our own motion, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and vacate our December 14, 2004, order. A final order in this matter will be issued as of this date, incorporating by reference the text of the attached vacated order.
 
*2 ORDER: The proceedings are reopened, the respondent’s motion to reissue is granted, and our December 14, 2004, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated December 14, 2004, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 2400991 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAMOUN MOHAMMAD AL BAJAH
File: AXX XX5 734 - Los Angeles, CA
May 27, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alan Diamante, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered deported from the United States on September 16, 1993, because he failed to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on July 25,2007, but the Immigration Judge denied it on September 21, 2007. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that his failure to appear for his hearing was caused by ineffective assistance of counsel, and he filed his motion with due diligence after becoming aware that he was the victim of such. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002). The respondent indicates in his affidavit that he received the Order to Show Cause, but he did not receive the Notice of Hearing because he changed his address and did not notify the Immigration Court of the change. He indicated that he hired Enrique Zuniga to assist him with his immigration status, and spoke with Mr. Zuniga after he received the charging document. According to the respondent, Mr. Zuniga told the respondent that he need not appear for a hearing, and he would take care of everything. The respondent states that he did not hear from Mr. Zuniga, and he tried to consult with him. He indicates that he was unable to contact him in 1993 despite repeated attempts. The respondent does not indicate why he did not contact the Immigration Court to ascertain the status of his case. We will not equitably toll the period for seeking reopening to a time later than 1993, the point that the respondent tried to contact Mr. Zuniga because he apparently was concerned about his immigration status. The respondent by not filing a motion to reopen until July 25, 2007, did not exercise due diligence in these proceedings, and we do not find it appropriate to reopen them. The motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances was filed untimely.
 
We further note that the respondent indicates in his affidavit at paragraph 23 that he was aware in 2006 that he had been ordered deported in absentia. He does not indicate, however, the date in 2006 that he learned such information. He has therefore not established that he filed his motion to reopen on July 25, 2007, within 180 days of learning that he had been ordered deported in absentia. Wetherefore do not find it appropriate to conclude that the motion was timely filed even if we tolled the period for seeking reopening to the point that the respondent became aware of the September 16, 1993, order. Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090 (9th Cir. 2007). We will not equitably toll the period for reopening until the date that current counsel determined that the respondent may have been victim of professional negligence. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
David W. Crosland
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2400991 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2401078 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LILIA ANHEL GONZALEZ-MUNIZ
File: AXX XX4 926 - Los Angeles, CA
May 23, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriella Navarro-Busch, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Prepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; cancellation of removal; voluntary departure
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated March 21, 2007, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen these proceedings so that she may pursue an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1). The respondent, through counsel, has appealed. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s appeal.
 
With regards to the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Ninth Circuit has held that the failure to comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), will not bar consideration of a motion based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, where the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. See Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993, 998 (9th Cir. 2006). The factors set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are intended to ensure both that an adequate factual record exists for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding that ineffectiveness assistance was clear on the face of the record where counsel failed to file application for relief).
 
In this case, the respondent’s counsel was ineffective because she failed to timely file an application for cancellation of removal. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 900 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding ineffective assistance of counsel when counsel failed to file application for cancellation of removal by date imposed by Immigration Judge); Rodriguez-Lariz, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002)(finding ineffective assistance of counsel when counsel failed to timely file application for suspension of deportation). The respondent was presumptively prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance because it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). In this regard, the respondent’s motion includes an application for cancellation of removal and supporting documentation.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent may be afforded an opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal. At the reopened proceedings, both parties may present additional evidence on the issue of the respondent’s eligibility for relief from removal, and the Immigration Judge shall enter a new decision.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401078 (BIA)
End of Document

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(I)º -Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document

APPLICATION: Reconsider, reopen, remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on April 22, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s September 27, 2007, denial of her motion to reconsider.¿FN1º On April 28, 2008, the respondent filed what appears to be a motion to reconsider captioned “motion to withdraw the former attorney’s motion to reconsider improvidently filed and consider our motion to reopen filed on November 2007 that has not been reviewed.” The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
In her April 28, 2008, motion, the respondent alleges that she had filed a motion to reopen on November 17, 2007, based on ineffective assistance of counsel which the Board failed to consider in their April 22, 2008, decision.¿FN2º See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). On May 12, 2008, the respondent submitted additional documents which reveal that her former attorney had been issued an informal admonition by the Office of Bar Counsel in the District of Columbia on April 24, 2008, relating to her ineffective representation of the respondent before the Immigration Judge and the Board of Immigration Appeals. Based on our review of the record and the documents submitted by the respondent, we find it appropriate to reopen and remand proceedings to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondent the opportunity to apply for the relief that she seeks. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted. Also, proceedings are reopened and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2008 WL 2401093 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HAYDEN GAVIN EDWARDS
File: AXX XX9 005 - Bloomington, MN
May 21, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Fitzmore Harris, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel J. Pornschloegl
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This proceeding has been before the Immigration Judge and the Board on numerous previous occasions. In November 2006, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed, and the respondent filed a timely Notice of Appeal. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent submitted evidence that his conviction for attempted criminal sale of a controlled substance-crack cocaine-had been amended to attempted possession of a controlled substance. Based on the additional evidence submitted, on February 7, 2007, the Board remanded the record for further examination of the respondent’s removability and eligibility for cancellation of removal. During most of his initial proceeding before the Immigration Judge, as well as during the first appeal, the respondent was represented by Melvin Duke.
 
After the Board remanded proceedings, the respondent hired new counsel, Dewey Nelson, to represent him (Tr. dated 3/8/07, 3/15/07, and 3/22/07). In an oral decision issued March 22, 2007, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), based on the amended conviction,¿FN1º and denied all relief. For purposes of the appeal, the respondent again retained Mr. Duke. On June 28, 2007, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion. Our June 28, 2007, decision was our final administrative decision for purposes of appeal. Thereafter, on August 15, 2007, and January 3, 2008, the Board denied two more motions.
 
On February 11, 2008, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen. The motion is untimely, as well as number-barred, and the respondent has not demonstrated that a regulatory exception applies. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) and (3). The respondent alleges that the time and number limitations should be equitably tolled because Mr. Duke provided ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The fact that Mr. Duke was disbarred by the state of New York in 2000, and may not have been qualified to practice before the Immigration Courts or the Board, does not demonstrate per se ineffective assistance of counsel, or otherwise satisfy the requirements of equitable tolling, so as to to justify reopening sua sponte.¿FN2º
 
*2 To warrant equitable tolling, the respondent must show, inter alia, that he exercised due diligence during the period he seeks to have tolled. Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858 (8th Cir. 2006); Kanyi v. Gonzales, 406 F.3d 1087 (8th Cir. 2005). In the instant case, the respondent admits that he was concerned about Mr. Duke’s representation as of October 2006. The respondent was apparently so concerned about Mr. Duke’s representation that, after the Board remanded proceedings, he retained new counsel. He then, however, hired Mr. Duke again for purposes of filing another appeal, as well as filing motions to reopen. Since the respondent was on notice as early as October 2006 regarding Mr. Duke’s alleged ineffectiveness, and has not provided an adequate explanation, the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence in raising ineffective assistance of counsel at this late date.
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by Mr. Duke’s actions. The Immigration Judge held that the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, which provides, with certain exceptions not applicable here, that an alien convicted of a violation of (or a conspiracy or attempt to violate) any law or regulation of a State relating to a controlled substance as defined in 21 U.S.C. § 802, is inadmissible (I.J. Dec. dated 3/22/07 at 4). The record indicates that the respondent was convicted of attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fourth degree in violation of New York Penal Law § 220.09, Subsection 1 (Gp. Exh. 8, Tab A and Transcript of Plea Agreement). According to the transcript of the plea agreement, the narcotic drug involved was crack cocaine (Gp. Exh. 8, Transcript). See also Respondent’s Rebuttal filed 2/1/07, Exh. A). The respondent’s state conviction is a violation of a law relating to a controlled substance as defined in 21 U.S.C. 802. See 21 U.S.C. §§ 802(6) and (17); 21 U.S.C. §§ 812, Schedule II(a)(4); 21 U.S.C. § 846; NY. Penal Law, § 220.09 subs. 1; N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3306, Schedule II(b)(4). It is clear, therefore, that the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act.
 
The respondent contends that Mr. Duke should have contested removability because his certificate of disposition conviction documents indicate that he was convicted under New York Penal Law § 220.09.00, but § 220.09 does not include a subsection “00”, that the respondent did not admit to possessing crack cocaine, and the transcript of the plea agreement was not properly admitted. We disagree. The certificate of disposition indictment indicates that the respondent was convicted under § 220.09, not § 220.09.00. Further, the transcript of the plea agreement, which was admissible, clearly indicates that the respondent’s amended conviction was for crack cocaine under § 220.09 subsection 1 (Gp. Exh. 8, Transcript). See Shepard v. U.S., 544 U.S. 13, 16 (2005)(court may examine, inter alia, transcript of plea colloquy); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41.¿FN3º The transcript of the plea agreement was marked as evidence, and treated as admitted since the Immigration Judge relied on it in reaching his decision, and neither Mr. Nelson nor the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) objected.
 
*3 It is unclear, moreover, why current counsel is challenging the Immigration Judge’s reliance on the transcript of the plea agreement. Without it, the record of proceeding would include two convictions for controlled substances-a certificate of disposition conviction for attempted criminal possession, as well as for attempted criminal sale, because the certificate regarding the conviction for attempted criminal possession does not indicate that it amended the attempted criminal sale conviction. Moreover, the Federal Rules of Evidence do not apply in immigration proceedings, but to the extent such rules apply, the transcript’s authenticity cannot be questioned at this time because there is no question that the transcript of the plea agreement is what it was purported to be-the transcript of the respondent’s original plea and conviction for attempted criminal sale, his sentencing, and his amended conviction.¿FN4º Accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by Mr. Duke’s alleged ineffectiveness regarding removability.
 
Since the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, he was not eligible to adjust his status because there was no waiver available for inadmissability under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, except for simple possession of a small amount of marihuana, see section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and, accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice. With regard to voluntary departure, the respondent was not eligible for such relief while the proceeding was pending before the Immigration Judge. See section 240B(b)(l)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(B); section 101(f)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(3). Further, at that time, he was not represented by Mr. Duke, and there is no claim of ineffective assistance pending against Mr. Nelson.
 
Regarding the respondent’s argument that Mr. Duke failed to pursue naturalization once the respondent became eligible, the respondent has also not demonstrated prejudice. It is fundamental that Immigration Judges and the Board do not have the authority to determine whether an alien in proceedings is prima facie eligible for naturalization for purposes of terminating proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f). See Matter of Hidalgo, 24 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2007)(Because the Board of Immigration Appeals and the Immigration Judges lack jurisdiction to adjudicate applications for naturalization, removal proceedings may only be terminated pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f) where the DHS has presented an affirmative communication attesting to an alien’s prima facie eligibility for naturalization.). See also Matter of Cruz, 15 I&N Dec. 236 (BIA 1975). An applicant bears the burden of demonstrating that he has been a person of good moral character, and the DHS may consider conduct and behavior beyond the statutorily prescribed period. 8 C.F.R. § 316.10. The respondent filed his application for naturalization with DHS on or about November 30, 2007. As of today’s date, the respondent has not presented evidence that he has obtained an affirmative communication attesting to his prima facie eligibility from the DHS, and, therefore, cannot demonstrate prejudice. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*4 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2

We further note that Mr. Duke did not notify either the Board or the Immigration Judge that he had been disbarred in New York, even though the Notice of Appearance forms request such information. In January 2008, Mr. Duke was suspended from practicing before the Board or Immigration Courts.

2008 WL 2401042 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHARLES NDUBUISI IGWENAGU A.K.A. CHARLES N. IGWEMAGUE
File: AXX XX1 639 - Miami, FL
May 20, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Zareefa Khan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated April 1, 2008. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed any opposition to the respondent’s current motion, and we therefore deem the current motion to be unopposed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reconsider requires an assertion by the moving party that the original decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See generally Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 404 (BIA 1991); 8C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Initially, we note that in support of his motion, the respondent has cited cases arising outside the Eleventh Circuit. However, this Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit. See Matter of Olivares, 23 I&N Dec. 148, 149 (BIA 2001), citing Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989). Thus, the cases cited by the respondent arising outside the Eleventh Circuit are not controlling.
 
In the instant case, the respondent has failed to present additional legal arguments which could not have been made during our earlier review of this matter on April 1, 2008. Additionally, the respondent appears to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, a petitioner attempting to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must at least “substantially” comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the requirements a respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); see also Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003). In the instant matter, the respondent has failed to do so. The respondent has not identified any change in the law affecting his appeal nor has he identified an aspect which we overlooked during our prior review on April 1, 2008. Having considered the respondent’s contentions, we are not persuaded to disturb our decision dated April 1, 2008, for the reasons stated therein.
 
*2 Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL BARBOSA DA COSTA JEAN RAFAEL DA COSTA MICHELE ASSUMPCAO DA COSTA THIAGO ASSUMPCAO COSTA NOEULUIZ COSTA
Files: AXX XX2 205 - Miami, FL
AXX XX4 617
AXX XX4 618
AXX XX4 619
AXX XX4 620
May 20, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents, natives and citizens of Brazil, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision of September 20, 2006. We will accept this appeal by certification pursuant to our authority in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). See generally Matter of Liadov, 23 I&N Dec. 999 (BIA 2006). However, the respondents* appeal will be dismissed on the merits.
 
As an initial matter, we note that the respondents have submitted new evidence on appeal, including a letter from the First Brazilian Baptist Church of South Florida dated October 2005, a pamphlet from the Community Blood Centers of South Florida (CBCSF) indicating that Elaine Hernandez, who is of unknown relation to the respondents, gave blood on October 24, 2006, and two printouts from a Florida Department of State website (www.sunbiz.org) relating to Paint by Manny and Prachel Painting and Waterproofing.
 
This Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). Accordingly, we cannot consider any new evidence presented for the first time on appeal, and any party asserting that this Board cannot properly resolve an appeal without further fact-finding must file a motion to remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). In this case, even if we construe the respondents’ submission as a motion to remand, the new evidence that the respondents seek to offer is either immaterial, as in the case of the CBCSF pamphlet, or was previously available and could have been discovered or presented at the former hearing on September 20, 2006, as in the case of the church letter and the printouts from www.sunbiz.org. See C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, we will adjudicate the appeal based upon the evidence contained in the record when it was before the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal, the respondents argue that they received ineffective assistance of counsel from the seven attorneys who represented them at various junctures during the last 10 years. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, in order to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish that his or her counsel’s performance was deficient to the point that it impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing such that the alien was unable to reasonably present his or her case. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273 (11th Cir.2005). In addition, the alien must establish that he or she suffered prejudice as a result of counsel’s ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 1274.
 
*2 In this case, the respondents have not complied with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements. They have not provided detailed affidavits setting forth the agreements that they reportedly entered into with each of their seven attorneys. They have not informed the individuals whose integrity or competence is being impugned of the allegations leveled against them and given them an opportunity to respond. Nor have they indicated whether complaints have been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. Although the respondents present a sympathetic case, they have failed entirely to comply with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements, which the Eleventh Circuit has determined are not unduly onerous. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir.2003). Accordingly, the respondents’ appeal is dismissed, and their motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE LUIS DIAZ-ESPINOZA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on February 1, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. At that time, we found that the Immigration Judge appropriately denied the respondent’s morion to reopen his in absentia removal proceedings.
 
On January 24, 2008, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit remanded the respondent’s case to this Board to consider whether the respondent’s declaration of non-receipt of the hearing notice rebutted the presumption of effective service, citing Sembiring v. Gonzales, 499 F. 3d 981 (9th Cir. 2007) (unsworn written statement of non-receipt of a hearing notice may be sufficient to rebut the weaker presumption of delivery of a notice sent by regular mail). See also Salta v. INS, 314 F.3d 1076 (9th Cir. 2002). The Court also remanded the case for the Board to consider whether the respondent’s counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance was an exceptional circumstance within the meaning of 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).
 
We find that such determination requires further factual findings. In view of the Court’s order, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge to address the respondent’s claim of a lack of notice of his removal hearing and ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
ORDER: The Board’s order dated February 1, 2006, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the order of the court and this opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Juan P. Osuna

2008 WL 2401148 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO SUAREZ DOMINGUEZ
File: AXX XX8 214 - San Antonio, TX
May 15, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin Adams
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Rescission
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s June 26, 2007, decision denying his motion to reopen the Immigration Judge’s April 9, 2007, decision ordering him removed in absentia. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The appeal is dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent avers the Immigration Judge failed to consider that he did not appear at his hearing as he was misled by his attorney (Notice of Appeal). He maintains former counsel failed to inform him that it was possible a pending motion to change venue could be denied, and the respondent was required to appear at his scheduled hearing (Resp.’s Br.). The respondent contends he had no reason to avoid his hearing, and asks that his case be reopened.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was essentially based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As the Immigration Judge correctly noted, the respondent did not comply with any of the requirements for such a claim, as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Such a motion should be supported by 1) an affidavit setting forth “in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken,” as well as any representations made by counsel to the alien; 2) proof that the movant has informed former counsel of the allegations in writing, as well as any response received; and 3) a statement detailing “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Id. As the respondent’s motion did not include any of the noted evidence, the Immigration Judge did not err in finding that reopening was not warranted.
 
For the respondent’s benefit, we further note it is well-established that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986). A respondent should not assume that a motion for a change of venue will be granted. Matter of Rivera, supra.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401148 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2401185 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EJIRO OMOKURU
File: AXX XX8 453 - Newark, NJ
May 13, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tahir Mella, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen her removal proceedings completed by final, administrative order on February 25, 2003, when an Immigration Judge granted the respondent the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States and entered an alternate order of removal in the event that the respondent failed to depart as promised. The instant motion, filed with this Board on October 9, 2007, is the respondent’s third motion to reopen. Her prior motions to reopen were denied. The instant motion to reopen, filed with this Board on October 9, 2007, will also be denied.
 
The respondent argues that, subsequent to failing to voluntarily depart the United States as required, she received ineffective assistance from her former counsel when seeking to reopen her proceedings because he did not timely seek reopening in order that she could apply for discretionary relief from removal on the basis of her marriage to a United States citizen.¿FN1º The respondent’s motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of her former counsel is without factual and legal merit.
 
The respondent’s failure to voluntarily depart the United States as required on May 26, 2003, rendered her statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status on the basis of her marriage to a United States citizen pursuant to the operation of section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8U.S.C. § 1229c)(d). The reason for the respondent’s failure to voluntarily depart the United States as promised is irrelevant as no exception to the limitation on discretionary relief under section 240B(d) exists for failures to depart due to circumstances beyond the alien’s control. Having asserted that she would voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent could choose, for whatever reason, to not abide by her promise, but she remains subject to the consequences of her actions. See generally Ngarurih v. Ashcroft, 371 F.3d 182, 194 (4th Cir. 2004) (Noting that voluntary departure is, from beginning to end, voluntary. Even if an alien requests the relief and obtains it, the alien may later reject it by overstaying the period specified for departure. However, if the alien rejects voluntary departure in this manner, then the alien remains subject to the real consequences of that action, including a monetary penalty and subjection to the ordinary bars on subsequent relief).
 
Thus, respondent’s former counsel was not ineffective in failing to apprise the Immigration Judge and this Board of the respondent’s marriage in conjunction with the filing of her earlier motion to reopen as the respondent’s marriage was not material to the issue of reopening. The respondent could not have sought relief from removal on the basis of her marriage as she was ineligible to adjust her status on the basis of her marriage due to her failure to voluntarily depart the United States as promised. Inasmuch as the instant motion fails to establish that the respondent’s former counsel was ineffective in failing to apprise this Board of the respondent’s marriage to a United States citizen, the instant motion to reopen is appropriately denied.
 
*2 Similarly, the regulatory time limitation on seeking reopening before the Immigration Judge had already expired when the respondent first sought reopening of her removal proceeding. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).¿FN2º Thus, the respondent has failed to establish that her former attorney was ineffective in failing to file a timely motion to reopen in this matter because the instant motion does not establish that he was retained to file a motion to reopen at a time when such a motion could reasonably have been timely filed.
 
Furthermore, the instant motion is barred by both the time and number limitations on reopening set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent was aware of all of the material facts raised in support of the instant motion on October 1, 2004, when this Board denied a prior motion to reopen filed by the respondent in this matter as untimely. The instant motion was filed over 3 years later. The respondent’s motion does not establish that she acted with due diligence in raising her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Thus, her motion is also appropriately denied because it has not been filed within a reasonable time from the respondent becoming aware of the material facts raised in support of the motion.
 
In addition, the instant motion fails to establish the respondent’s prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status based upon her marriage because the respondent did not voluntarily depart the United States as required. See Section 240B(d) of the Act. She may not adjust her status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1225, for a period of 10 years from her failure to voluntarily depart the United States as required on May 26, 2003. Therefore, she is not prima facie eligible for the relief she is requesting should her proceedings be reopened and her motion is appropriately denied on this basis’ as well. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied because she was and is not eligible to adjust her status due to her failure to voluntarily depart the United States as promised and her former attorney did not, therefore, render ineffective assistance by failing to timely pursue reopening in order that the respondent might apply for adjustment of status on the basis of her marriage to a United States citizen.
 
David W. Crosland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion does not allege or establish that she entered into an agreement with her former counsel to file a motion to reopen on her behalf at any time prior to her failure to voluntarily depart the United States as promised by May 26, 2003. Indeed, her complaint filed against former counsel with state disciplinary authorities sets forth events occurring in June 2003 and asserts that she “later” contacted her former attorney to discuss her deportation order and the possibility of reopening her case based upon her marriage to a United States citizen.

2

This Board’s decision dated October 1, 2004, indicating that any motion to reopen would have been due on or before June 29, 2004, is erroneous. This Board’s decision dated October 1, 2004, mistakenly applied the time limitation on reopening from the date of this Board’s earlier decision upholding the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s first motion to reopen. The time limitation on reopening does not run from an administratively final order denying reopening but, rather, from the administratively final order completing the respondent’s removal proceedings. Thus, in this case, a timely motion to reopen could only have been filed by the respondent on or before May 27, 2003. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).

2008 WL 2401060 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IHEANYI ONWUKA
File: AXX XX5 091 - Houston, TX
May 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda L. Walker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated March 13, 2008. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent argues for the first time in an attachment to his motion for reconsideration that his failure to meet the 90-day filing period for submitting a motion to reopen was due to “wrong advice from ¿hisº previous lawyer.” He further states that he consulted the lawyer on July 17, 2007, 5 days after the Immigration Judge’s July 12 2007, decision, and was advised to “wait until ¿heº received another communication from the Immigration Office, which should arrive in about 90 days.”
 
“A motion to reconsider is a request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked. A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard.” Matter of O-S-G, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57 (BIA 2006) (citations omitted). To the extent that the respondent is raising a claim of ineffective assistance by his former counsel, he has failed to comply with any of the requirements for asserting such a claim outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).¿FN1º See Lara v. Trominiski, 216 F.3d 383, 389 (5th Cir. 2000) (general application of the Lozada requirements does not constitute an abuse of discretion by the Board). The respondent failed to submit an affidavit outlining in full his contacts with his prior attorney, and failed to provide his former counsel with an opportunity to respond. It is unclear what correspondence the respondent would have been waiting for after the Immigration Judge issued a final decision in this case, nor has the respondent indicated whether he was aware of, or was explained, the filing requirements for submitting a motion to reopen. Moreover, we find that the respondent could and should have addressed the timeliness issue during his appeal. While the respondent states in his motion for reconsideration, through counsel, that he just realized that an October 23, 2007, letter addressed to the Immigration Judge, which outlined his timeliness argument, was sent to the wrong address, he could and should have argued this issue in his appeal brief to the Board. We therefore find that the respondent has failed to establish a basis for equitable tolling of the statutory filing period. See Coleman v. Johnson, 148 F,3d 398, 402 (5th Cir. 1999) (“A garden variety claim of excusable neglect does not support equitable tolling.”); Davis v. Johnson, 158 D3d 806, 811 (5th Cir. 1998) (Equitable tolling is permitted only “in rare and exceptional circumstances.”). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

2008 WL 7540259 (BIA)
